Fourth Argument: Abrogation Implies Temporal Origin
Sanusi presents a brief deductive argument linking abrogation to temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of abrogation serves as a rational proof that Allah's command is temporal and not eternal.

Core Idea
Abrogation proves commands are temporal because what can cease cannot be eternal.

This lesson presents the fourth rational argument from classical kalam: the concept of abrogation (naskh) demonstrates the temporal origin of divine commands. By analyzing its definitions and implications, we see that a ruling which can be removed or ended cannot be eternal, thus refuting the view that Allah's commands are uncreated and pre-eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise, deductive structure: a premise (abrogation is permissible), definitions, and a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the two definitions of abrogation
The passage defines abrogation in two ways: removal of a ruling after its establishment, or expiry of its time period. Both definitions share the idea that the ruling ceases to exist after having existed.
Recognize that regardless of which definition is used, the key point is cessation.
3
Trace the logical implication
The conclusion follows: if a ruling can cease to exist, it cannot be eternal (qadim). Therefore, the divine command subject to abrogation must be temporal (hadith). This is a classic kalam argument from contingency.
The argument relies on the consensus that abrogation is permissible for divine commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن النسخ عندهم جائز ، والنسخ عبارة عن رفع الحكم بعد ثبوته . أو عن انتهاء زمن ذلك الحكم . وعلى التقديرين فقد عدم بعد وجوده ، وما يثبت عدمه امتنع قدمه
Fourth: Abrogation (al-naskh) is permissible according to them, and abrogation is the removal of a ruling after its establishment, or the expiry of the time of that ruling. In both cases, it ceases to exist after its existence, and whatever is subject to ceasing to exist, its eternity is impossible.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth rational argument: the permissibility of abrogation (al-naskh) proves that Allah's command is temporal. Abrogation is defined as either the lifting of a ruling after it has been established, or the end of the time period of that ruling. In either case, the ruling ceases to exist after it had existed. But the eternal (al-qadim) cannot cease to exist. Therefore, since the ruling can be abrogated, it cannot be eternal. The argument relies on the theological consensus that abrogation is valid for divine commands, which implies change and temporality.

Key Terms
نسخ
حكم
ثبوت
عدم
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation contradicts Allah's knowledge because it implies a change of mind.
Correction: Abrogation does not imply change in Allah's knowledge; it pertains to the temporal nature of the command itself, not His knowledge. The argument is about the command's temporality, not divine ignorance.

Checkpoint

C) Abrogation is a human invention, not applicable to Allah.
D) Abrogation proves the command was created ex nihilo.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of abrogation proves that divine commands are temporal because what can cease to exist cannot be eternal.

Abrogation proves that God's commands are tailored to time and circumstance. Does your obedience reflect trust in God's perfect timing, or do you complain about the demands? Respond to God's command today with the awareness that it is a temporal gift, not a burden.

The Fifth Argument: Eternal Command Necessitates Contradiction
Shows that an eternal command would have to apply to all things, causing contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument against an eternal divine command uses the necessity of an eternal attribute's relation to conclude that it would lead to impossible contradiction.

Core Idea
An eternal command would have to command and forbid everything, which is impossible.

This passage presents a key argument against the eternity of God's command. It uses the principle that an eternal attribute necessarily relates to all possible objects. If God's command were eternal, it would have to apply to everything, leading to a contradiction. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a premise about eternal attributes, a conditional statement, and a conclusion of impossibility.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with the claim that an eternal attribute (al-sifa al-qadima) necessarily relates to all its possible objects. This is a foundational Kalam principle.
If the command is eternal, its relation to everything it could possibly command becomes necessary.
3
Understand the consequence
Given the denial of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyan), anything can be commanded and anything can be prohibited. So the eternal command would relate to all things, and the eternal prohibition would also relate to all things.
This yields a contradiction: everything is both commanded and forbidden.
4
Grasp the impossibility
The conjunction of two opposites (ijtima' al-diddayn) is impossible. Therefore, the command cannot be eternal. This refutes the claim that God's command is an eternal attribute.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الصفة القديمة الأزلية تكون تعلقاتها بمتعلقاتها أمراً ذاتياً لازماً واجباً . فلو كان أمر الله قديماً لوجب تعلقه بكل ما يصح تعلقه به ، لكن الحسن والقبح العقليين باطل عند القائلين بهذا القول ، فلا شيء إلا ويصح الأمر به ، ولا شيء إلا ويصح عنه ، فيلزم تعلق الأمر بكل الأشياء ، وتعلق النهي بكل الأشياء [؟]، فيلزم كون الأشياء بأسرها مأموراً بها ، منهياً عنها ، وذلك يوجب اجتماع الضدين ، وهو محال .
Fifth: The eternal, pre-eternal attribute has its relations to its objects as an essential, necessary, and obligatory matter. So if Allah's command were eternal, its relation to everything to which it could be related would be necessary. But rational good and evil are invalid according to the proponents of this view, so there is nothing except that it is possible to command it, and nothing except that it is possible to forbid it. Thus, the command would relate to all things, and the prohibition would relate to all things, resulting in all things being both commanded and forbidden, which necessitates the conjunction of two opposites, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument: an eternal attribute (al-sifa al-qadima al-azaliyya) necessarily relates to all its possible objects in a necessary and essential way. If Allah's command (amr) were eternal, it would have to relate to every possible object of command. But according to those who deny rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyan), anything can be commanded and anything can be prohibited. Consequently, the command would apply to all things, and the prohibition would also apply to all things, making everything both commanded and prohibited — a conjunction of opposites (ijtima' al-diddayn), which is impossible. Therefore, the command cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
صفة قديمة
تعلق
حسن وقبح
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies if one believes in objective moral values, because it denies rational good/evil.
Correction: Actually, the argument explicitly targets those who deny rational good/evil (the Ash'ari view). It uses their own premise that anything can be commanded to show that eternal command leads to contradiction. It is not an argument against objective morality, but a reductio of eternal command given the denial of objective morality.

Checkpoint
What absurd result does the fifth argument derive from assuming an eternal command?
A) The command would be independent of God's will.
B) The command would be both eternal and temporal.
C) Everything would be both commanded and prohibited, a contradiction.
D) The command would have no relation to creation.

Key Takeaway
An eternal command cannot exist because it would necessitate commanding and prohibiting everything, an impossible contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever presumed that because God's command is authoritative, it must be eternal and unchanging, even while ignoring the logical consequences? Reflect on your own understanding of God's attributes: do you truly accept that God's command is temporal and responsive to creation, or do you subconsciously treat it as static? How does this affect how you seek His guidance in your daily decisions?
?
This argument shows that an eternal command leads to contradiction unless there are inherent moral boundaries. But do you live as if morality is entirely arbitrary based on what God commands, or do you recognize that God's wisdom underlies His commands? Examine your heart: when you obey, do you do so because you trust that His commands are good, or merely because they are commanded? How would your obedience change if you believed God commands and forbids everything simultaneously?
?
The impossibility of an eternal command forces you to affirm that God's attribute of command is temporal—meaning God acts in time. Does this unsettle your conception of God's immutability? How can you reconcile God's immutability with His temporal commands? Search your own soul: do you hold God at a distance as an unchanging force, or do you engage with a living God who speaks and commands in history? What step will you take today to move from theoretical acceptance to living relationship with the commanding God?

The Sixth Argument: Change in Speech Proves Its Temporality
Sixth rational argument comparing knowledge and speech to prove speech is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth rational argument uses the analogy of changing knowledge to demonstrate that divine speech is temporal and cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Change in speech proves it is not eternal.

This passage presents the final argument from the first set of rational proofs. It draws a comparison between knowledge and speech: just as knowledge changes with its objects, speech changes with what it reports. Anything subject to change cannot be eternal, leading to the conclusion that divine speech is created in time, not coeternal with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the analogy and the conclusion.
The passage has a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the analogy
Find where the author compares knowledge and speech. Understand the parallel: both change when their objects change.
The analogy assumes that knowledge changes (a premise the opponent may accept).
3
Understand the principle of change
The author states that anything subject to change cannot be eternal. Reflect on why this is so: the eternal cannot cease to exist.
The Arabic word 'مالاً' is unclear; it likely means 'impossible' or 'non-existence'.
4
Note the conclusion
The conclusion explicitly states that both talab (demand) and khabar (report) cannot be eternal, regardless of whether talab is identified with will.
This wraps up the six arguments against the eternity of speech.
5
Avoid the common misunderstanding
Remember that the argument does not deny that Allah's knowledge is eternal; it only uses knowledge as an analogy to prove speech is temporal.
The focus is on speech, not knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إنا كما بينا : أن العلم بالشيء ، يجب أن يتغير عند تغير المعلومات ، فكذلك الخبر عن الشيء يتغير عند تغير المخبر عنه ، وكل ما يتطرق التغير إليه وجب أن لا يكون قديماً ، لأنه ثبت أن ما كان قديماً كان العدم عليه مالاً [؟] . فهذه وجوه عقلية ظاهرة في أنه يمتنع كون هذا الطلب والخبر قديماً، سواء قلنا: إن هذا الطلب هو الإرادة أو غيرها .
Sixth: Just as we have shown that knowledge of a thing must change when the known things change, likewise the report (khabar) about a thing changes when the thing reported about changes. And everything that is subject to change must not be eternal, because it has been established that what is eternal cannot be subject to non-existence [؟]. These are evident rational proofs that it is impossible for this demand (talab) and report (khabar) to be eternal, whether we say that this demand is the will or something else.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final argument from the first set, followed by a general conclusion. The argument draws an analogy: just as Allah's knowledge (al-'ilm) changes when the objects of knowledge change (a position the opponent might hold regarding temporal objects), so too does His speech (al-khabar) change when the object of the report changes. Anything subject to change cannot be eternal, because the eternal cannot cease to exist. The text then concludes: 'These are evident rational proofs that it is impossible for this demand (al-talab) and report (al-khabar) to be eternal, whether we say this demand is the will or something else.' This wraps up the six arguments against the eternity of Allah's speech.

Key Terms
علم
خبر
تغير
طلب
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that Allah's knowledge is not eternal.
Correction: The argument only uses knowledge as an analogy; it does not claim that knowledge itself is temporal. It concludes that speech is temporal, not knowledge.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the sixth argument use to prove that divine speech is not eternal?
A) Speech is like knowledge: both change with objects, so speech is temporal.
B) Speech is like power: both are attributes of perfection.
C) Speech is like will: both are eternal.
D) Speech is like action: both are created in time.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech changes with its objects and is therefore temporal, not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that divine speech changes to address each situation. Are you living as if Allah's words are a fixed history or as a living conversation that speaks to your current state? What will you do today to engage with His speech as if it is being revealed for this moment?
?
If speech is temporal because it adapts, then Allah's commands are not distant decrees but direct responses to your reality. Does your obedience reflect that you believe He is speaking specifically to you now, or do you treat His words as general advice? What habit will you change to take His commands more personally?
?
The analogy with knowledge shows that change is not a flaw but a sign of relevance. How does the temporality of speech impact your view of the Qur'an? Will you read it today as a timeless record or as a dynamic guide for your exact circumstances? What is one verse you will apply immediately?

First Argument for the Eternity of Allah's Speech
First argument for the eternity of Allah's speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first argument for the eternity of Allah's speech, which is based on the necessary connection between life and speech.

Core Idea
Life demands speech or its opposite; deficiency is impossible for Allah, so His speech is eternal.

The first argument for the eternity of Allah's speech is a simple but powerful syllogism. It begins with a common attribute of Allah – that He is living – and draws a necessary conclusion about His speech. The argument shows that the only alternative to speech is deficiency, and deficiency cannot be attributed to Allah. Consequently, speech must be an eternal attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the premises
The passage states three premises: Allah is living; every living being can be described as speaking; and for any attribute, a subject must be described by that attribute or its opposite. Identify each one in the text.
Pay attention to the logical connectors 'and' and 'so'.
2
Understand the concept of 'opposite'
The opposite of speech is given as muteness (al-khars) and silence (al-sukut). Both are considered deficiencies. Reflect on why silence is also considered an opposite of speech.
Muteness is the inability to speak; silence is the absence of speech.
3
Trace the conclusion
Since deficiency is impossible for Allah, and the alternative to speech is deficient, Allah must be described with speech. The conclusion specifies that this applies in pre-eternity (al-azal).
The argument establishes speech as an essential, not contingent, attribute.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument shows that speech is a necessary attribute of Allah, not something He acquires later. It is a key point in Islamic theology regarding the eternity of the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه تعالى حي ، وكل حي فإنه يصح أن يكون متكلماً ، وكل من صح عليه الاتصاف بصفة ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة أو بضدها . وضد الكلام هو الخرس والسكوت . وذلك نقص ، والنقص على الله تعالى محال ، فوجب أن يكون في الأزل موصوفاً بالكلام
First: He, exalted, is living, and every living being can be described as speaking. And everyone of whom it is possible to be described with an attribute must be described with that attribute or its opposite. The opposite of speech is muteness and silence. That is a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah, exalted. So it is necessary that He is described with speech in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the eternity of Allah's speech (kalam). The argument proceeds: Allah is living (hayy). Every living being can possibly be described as speaking. For any attribute that can be predicated of a subject, the subject must be described either by that attribute or by its opposite (since one cannot be free of both). The opposite of speech is muteness (al-khars) and silence (al-sukut), which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for Allah. Therefore, Allah must be described with speech in pre-eternity (fi al-azal). The argument establishes that speech is an essential attribute of Allah, not a contingent one.

Key Terms
حي
متكلم
خرس
سكوت
نقص
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think muteness is the only opposite of speech, and silence is not considered an opposite.
Correction: The argument explicitly includes silence (al-sukut) as an opposite of speech, both being deficiencies.

Checkpoint
Why does the first argument conclude that Allah must have speech in pre-eternity?
A) Because Allah is powerful, and power requires speech.
B) Because every living being must have speech or its opposite, and the opposite (muteness/silence) is a deficiency, which cannot apply to Allah.
C) Because Allah is wise, and wisdom necessitates speech.
D) Because speech is a created attribute, so it must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the eternity of Allah's speech shows that because Allah is living and deficiency is impossible, His speech must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's speech is eternal, how should that affect your reverence when reciting the Qur'an? Will you commit to reciting at least one verse today with the conscious awareness that these are the words of the Living One who has always spoken?
?
The argument says deficiency is impossible for Allah. When you complain about your circumstances, are you not implying that Allah has failed to give you what you deserve? What will you do this week to replace a complaint with gratitude?
?
Since Allah is living and must be described with speech, do you live as if you are in constant dialogue with Him? When was the last time you paused to listen to His speech (the Qur'an) with your heart, not just your mind? Write down one specific change you will make to prioritize listening to Allah's speech daily.

The Second Argument: Speech Cannot Be Temporal in Any Locus
Proves speech is eternal by eliminating all possibilities for its temporal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the eternity of Allah's speech, which eliminates all possible ways speech could be created.

Core Idea
Speech cannot be temporal because it cannot arise in God, in another, or without a locus.

This passage builds on the consensus that Allah is speaking. It then systematically eliminates every conceivable way that speech could be a created thing, leaving only the conclusion that it must be eternal. Understanding this argument helps clarify why kalam theology insists on the pre-eternity of the divine attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice how the argument is structured as a disjunction of possibilities.
2
Identify the three impossible possibilities
The passage lists three possibilities for temporal speech: occurring in God's essence, in another, or without a locus. Identify each and note how they are introduced.
These are the three divisions the argument eliminates.
3
Understand why each possibility is ruled out
For each possibility, the author gives a reason why it is impossible. Read the commentary to grasp the logical basis for each rejection.
Pay attention to the analogy with ignorance and powerlessness.
4
Connect to the conclusion
After eliminating all three, the argument concludes that speech cannot be temporal, so it must be eternal. Reflect on how this supports the doctrine of the Quran's uncreatedness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : وهو أنا أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم ، فأما أن يكون متكلماً لذاته - وهو باطل بالاتفاق - أو يكون متكلماً بالكلام ، وذلك الكلام إن كان حادثاً ، فأما أن يحدث في ذاته، أو في غيره ، أو لا في محل : والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فبطل كون الكلام حادثاً ، فوجب أن يكون قديماً . وإنما قلنا : إن حدوث الكلام في ذات الله محال ، لأنه يوجب قيام الحوادث بذات الله وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إن حدوث الكلام في غيره محال ، لأنه لو جاز أن يكون متكلماً بكلام حاصل في غيره، لجاز أن يكون جاهلاً بجهل يقوم بغيره، وعاجزاً بعجز يقوم بغيره . وذلك باطل . وإنما قلنا : إن حدوث الكلام لا في محل : محال . بالاتفاق. وأيضاً : فكلام الله تعالى صفة ، وصفة الشيء تكون حاصلة فيه لا محالة
Second: We are agreed that He, exalted, is speaking (mutakallim). So either He is speaking by His essence — which is invalid by consensus — or He is speaking by speech. And if that speech is temporal, then either it occurs in His essence, or in another, or not in a locus. The three divisions are invalid, so speech being temporal is invalid, so it must be eternal. We say: speech occurring in God's essence is impossible because it entails temporal events subsisting in God's essence, which is impossible. We say: speech occurring in another is impossible because if it were permissible for Him to be speaking by speech subsisting in another, then it would be permissible for Him to be ignorant by ignorance subsisting in another, and powerless by powerlessness subsisting in another. That is invalid. We say: speech occurring not in a locus is impossible by consensus. Also, the speech of Allah is an attribute, and an attribute of a thing necessarily subsists in it.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the eternity of Allah's speech. It begins with the consensus that Allah is described as speaking (mutakallim). There are two possibilities: either He is speaking by His essence (i.e., He is speech itself) — which is invalid by agreement — or He is speaking by speech (kalam). If that speech is temporal (hadith), then it must either: (1) occur in God's essence (i.e., inhere in Him), (2) occur in another being, or (3) occur without a locus. The argument then eliminates each: (1) is impossible because it would mean temporal events (hawadith) subsist in God, which is not allowed; (2) is impossible because if speech could subsist in another, then other attributes like ignorance and powerlessness could also subsist in another, leading to absurdity; (3) is impossible by consensus, since an attribute must have a subject. Additionally, speech is an attribute of Allah, and an attribute must subsist in the one described. Therefore, speech must be eternal (qadim).

Key Terms
متكلم
كلام
حادث
قديم
محل
قيام الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think speech could be created in God without affecting His essence, but the argument holds that temporal attributes cannot subsist in God.
Correction: The argument shows that if speech were temporal and in God's essence, that would mean temporal events (hawadith) subsist in God, which is impossible. So speech cannot be created in God.

Checkpoint
What are the three impossible possibilities for temporal speech according to the second argument?
A) In God's essence, in another, or without a locus.
B) In God's essence, in the Preserved Tablet, or in the mind.
C) In the soul, in the world, or in language.
D) In angels, in prophets, or in scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The second argument proves that Allah's speech must be eternal because it cannot arise in any possible locus of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated, then the Qur'an is not a mere historical document but the very words of Allah that have always existed. When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel the weight of speaking eternal words? How would your awe and reverence change if you truly internalized that each verse you utter has been with Allah forever? Commit today to recite even one ayah with the conviction that it is eternal.
?
The argument proves that speech cannot be attributed to Allah if it arises in another. This means that the Qur'an is directly from Him, not mediated by a created message. Do you treat the Qur'an as a direct conversation from your Lord, or do you read it as a distant text? How can you increase your sense of intimate dialogue with Allah through His eternal speech? Set a specific time daily to read the Qur'an as if He is speaking to you personally.
?
The impossibility of temporal events in Allah's essence means that His attributes are perfect and unchanging. Does your concept of Allah allow for any change or development in His nature? If not, how does that affect your trust in His promises and decrees? Reflect on a time when you doubted Allah's consistency; how does this truth about His eternal attributes strengthen your reliance on Him? Make a firm resolve to trust in Allah's unchanging nature in a specific area of your life today.

Third Argument: Speech as Perfection Attribute
Proves speech is eternal by categorizing it as an attribute of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dichotomy of perfection vs. deficiency proves that Allah's speech must be eternal.

Core Idea
If speech were not eternal, Allah would lack perfection — absurd for God.

This lesson presents the third argument for the eternity of divine speech. It uses a simple but powerful logical dichotomy: speech is either an attribute of perfection or deficiency. By examining both possibilities, the argument shows that only the eternal perfection option is consistent with Allah's nature. Read the passage to see the reasoning unfold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
Read the first line and note that speech is categorized as either perfection or deficiency. This is the starting point.
2
Examine the perfection branch
If speech is perfection, it must be eternal: otherwise Allah would lack perfection, which is a deficiency — impossible.
3
Examine the deficiency branch
If speech is deficiency, Allah cannot have it at all — deficiency cannot be attributed to Him.
4
Apply the agreed fact
We all agree that Allah has spoken (e.g., revealed scriptures). So speech cannot be a deficiency.
5
Conclude
Therefore speech is a perfection attribute, and thus must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إن الكلام إما أن يكون صفة كمال ، أو صفة نقص . فإن كان صفة كمال وجب أن يكون موصوفاً به أبداً . إذ لو لم يكن موصوفاً به في الأزل لزم كونه خالياً عن صفة الكمال ، والخلو عن صفة الكمال نقصان، والنقصان على الله محال . وإذا كان صفة نقص وجب أن لا يتصف به البتة ، لأن النقص على الله - عز وجل - محال. وحيث توافقنا على أنه تعالى قد اتصف به ، علمنا أنه ليس من صفات النقص
Third: Speech is either an attribute of perfection or an attribute of deficiency. If it is an attribute of perfection, then He must be described with it eternally. For if He were not described with it in pre-eternity, it would follow that He is devoid of the attribute of perfection, and being devoid of an attribute of perfection is a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. If it is an attribute of deficiency, then He must not be described with it at all, because deficiency is impossible for Allah, mighty and majestic. Since we agree that He has described Himself with it, we know that it is not from the attributes of deficiency.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the eternity of Allah's speech. It uses a dichotomy: speech must be either an attribute of perfection (sifat kamal) or an attribute of deficiency (sifat naqs). If it is an attribute of perfection, then Allah must be described with it eternally — otherwise, He would lack perfection, which is a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. If it is an attribute of deficiency, then He must not be described with it at all, because deficiency cannot be attributed to Allah. Since we all agree that Allah describes Himself as speaking (e.g., in the Qur'an), speech cannot be an attribute of deficiency; therefore, it is an attribute of perfection and must be eternal.

Key Terms
صفة كمال
صفة نقص
خلو
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that speech could be a perfection only if it is created, but the argument uses the dichotomy to force an eternal attribution.
Correction: Actually, the argument does not depend on speech being created; it argues that if speech is a perfection, it must be eternal because lacking it would be a deficiency.

Checkpoint
How does the third argument conclude that speech is eternal?
A) Speech is an attribute of deficiency, so it must be eternal.
B) Speech is an attribute of perfection, and lack of it would be a deficiency; since Allah has speech, it must be eternal.
C) Speech is an attribute of power, so it must be eternal.
D) Speech is an attribute of will, so it must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an attribute of perfection, so it must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument declares that deficiency is impossible for Allah. Yet, in your daily life, where do you make excuses for your own deficiencies—especially in your relationship with Allah's speech (neglecting recitation, not pondering its meaning, ignoring its commands)? What concrete step will you take this week to remove that deficiency?
?
If the perfection of Allah's speech demands that He always speaks, what does your silence—your lack of speaking His words or calling upon Him—say about your own state? Will you commit to speaking at least one verse or supplication from His eternal words every hour today?

First Proof: The Principle of Attribute and Opposite
First rational proof for pre-eternity of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech, which is based on the principle that any possible attribute must be possessed or its opposite, and since the opposite of speech is a deficiency, God must have speech pre-eternally.

Core Idea
If an attribute's opposite is a deficiency, God must possess the attribute eternally.

The first argument for the pre-eternity of speech relies on a foundational principle of attribute attribution: if a subject can validly be described with an attribute, it must either have that attribute or its opposite. Since God is living, speech is a valid attribute, and its opposite (muteness/silence) is a deficiency impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with speech in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument once, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, condition, conclusion.
2
Identify the key principle
The argument hinges on the rule: 'everyone for whom description with an attribute is valid, then it must be described with that attribute or its opposite.' Grasp this principle.
This is a general kalam principle.
3
Apply the principle to speech
Speech is valid for God because He is living. Its opposite is muteness/silence, which is a deficiency. Deficiencies are impossible for God, so He must have speech.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is described with speech in pre-eternity (i.e., His speech is pre-eternal). Ask yourself why this argument does not prove that God is always speaking in time.
It proves the attribute is pre-eternal, not necessarily its manifestation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم الكلام بأشياء:
الأول: إنه تعالى حي، وكل حي فإنه يصح أن يكون متكلماً، وكل من صح عليه الاتصاف بصفة، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة أو بضدها. وضد الكلام هو الخرس والسكوت. وذلك نقص، والنقص على الله تعالى محال، فوجب أن يكون في الأزل موصوفاً بالكلام.
والثاني: وهو أنا أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم، فأما أن يكون متكلماً لذاته - وهو باطل بالاتفاق - أو يكون متكلماً بالكلام، وذلك الكلام إن كان حادثاً، فأما أن يحدث في ذاته، أو في غيره، أو لا في محل: والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فبطل كون الكلام حادثاً، فوجب أن يكون قديماً. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في ذات الله محال، لأنه يوجب قيام الحوادث بذات الله وهو محال، وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في غيره محال، لأنه لو جاز أن يكون متكلماً بكلام حاصل في غيره، لجاز أن يكون جاهلاً بجهل يقوم بغيره، وعاجزاً بعجز يقوم بغيره. وذلك باطل. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام لا في محل: محال. فبالاتفاق. وأيضاً: فكلام الله تعالى صفة، وصفة الشيء تكون حاصلة فيه لا محالة.
والثالث: إن الكلام إما أن يكون صفة كمال، أو صفة نقص. فإن كان صفة كمال وجب أن يكون موصوفاً به أبداً. إذ لو لم يكن موصوفاً به في الأزل لزم كونه خالياً عن صفة الكمال، والخلو عن صفة الكمال نقصان، والنقصان على الله محال. وإذا كان صفة نقص وجب أن لا يتصف به البتة، لأن النقص على الله - عز وجل - محال. وحيث توافقنا على أنه تعالى قد اتصف به، علمنا أنه ليس من صفات النقص.
الرابع: إن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن المتكلم أكمل وأفضل ممن لم يكن متكلم، ولا شك أن الواحد منا متكلم. فلو لم يكن الله في الأزل متكلماً، لزم أن يكون حال الواحد منا عند وجوده أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى، حين كان في الأزل، وذلك محال. فيثبت أنه تعالى موصوف بالكلام في الأزل.
The proponents of the pre-eternity of speech argued with things: First: He, exalted, is living, and every living being is such that it is valid for it to be speaking, and everyone for whom description with an attribute is valid, then it must be described with that attribute or its opposite. The opposite of speech is muteness and silence. That is deficiency, and deficiency upon God, exalted, is impossible, so it is necessary that He be described with speech in pre-eternity. Second: That we have agreed that He, exalted, is speaking. Either He is speaking by His essence—which is invalid by consensus—or He is speaking by speech. That speech, if it were originated, then either it occurs in His essence, or in another, or not in a locus. The three divisions are invalid, so the occurrence of speech as originated is invalid, so it must be pre-eternal. We say: The occurrence of speech in the essence of God is impossible because it necessitates the occurrence of accidents in the essence of God, and that is impossible. The occurrence of speech in another is impossible because if it were permissible for Him to be speaking by speech subsisting in another, then it would be permissible for Him to be ignorant by ignorance subsisting in another, and powerless by powerlessness subsisting in another. That is invalid. The occurrence of speech not in a locus is impossible by consensus. Also, the speech of God, exalted, is an attribute, and the attribute of a thing occurs in it necessarily. Third: Speech is either an attribute of perfection or an attribute of deficiency. If it is an attribute of perfection, then it is necessary that He be described with it always. For if He were not described with it in pre-eternity, it would necessitate His being devoid of the attribute of perfection, and being devoid of the attribute of perfection is deficiency, and deficiency upon God is impossible. And if it is an attribute of deficiency, then it is necessary that He never be described with it, because deficiency upon God—mighty and majestic—is impossible. Since we have agreed that He, exalted, is described with it, we know that it is not from the attributes of deficiency. Fourth: Necessary knowledge exists that the speaker is more perfect and better than one who is not a speaker. There is no doubt that each one of us is a speaker. If God were not speaking in pre-eternity, then the state of each one of us at the time of his existence would be better and more perfect than God, exalted, when He was in pre-eternity, and that is impossible. So it is established that He, exalted, is described with speech in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents four rational arguments for the pre-eternity (qidam) of divine speech (kalam). The first argument is based on the principle that for any attribute that can be predicated, the subject must be described either by that attribute or its opposite. Since God is living, speaking is possible; its opposite (muteness/silence) is a deficiency, impossible for God, so He must be speaking in pre-eternity. The second argument uses a disjunctive syllogism: we agree God is speaking; either by essence (invalid by consensus) or by speech. That speech, if originated, must subsist in His essence, in another, or in no locus. All three are impossible: in His essence because it would entail accidents in God; in another because that would allow other deficiencies like ignorance to be attributed to God through an external substrate; without a locus by consensus. Also, an attribute must inhere in its subject. Therefore speech is pre-eternal. The third argument classifies speech as either perfection or deficiency. If perfection, God must always have it (absence of perfection is deficiency). If deficiency, He must never have it. Since He does have it, it is perfection, so He has it pre-eternally. The fourth argument uses comparative excellence: a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker. We are speakers; if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, we would be more perfect than the pre-eternal God, which is impossible.

Key Terms
كلام
خرس
سكوت
صفة كمال
صفة نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why must God be described with speech in pre-eternity?
A) Because God is living and speaking is necessary for life.
B) Because the opposite of speech, muteness, is a deficiency impossible for God.
C) Because if He were not speaking, He would be mute, which is a deficiency.
D) Because all living beings speak.

Key Takeaway
God must possess speech pre-eternally because its opposite is a deficiency and God cannot have deficiencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's speech is pre-eternal, perfect, and without deficiency. Yet you were created with the ability to speak, and you will be judged for every word. How will you change your use of speech today, knowing God's speech is the standard of perfection?
?
The proof uses the principle that an attribute's opposite is a deficiency, so God must have the perfect attribute. Where in your life have you settled for the 'opposite'—silence when you should speak truth, or careless speech when you should be silent? What concrete step will you take this week to align your speech with God's perfection?
?
If muteness (khurs) is a deficiency for God, does it not bother you that you often remain 'mute' about His greatness? What specific action will you take this week to make God's speech—the Qur'an—the subject of your daily conversation?

Second Proof: Disjunctive Syllogism on the Locus of Speech
Second rational proof using disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech, which uses a disjunctive syllogism to eliminate all possible ways speech could be originated, thus proving its pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If speech were created, it would have to subsist somewhere; all options are impossible, so speech must be pre-eternal.

The second argument builds on the consensus that God is speaking. If He speaks by an attribute, that attribute cannot be part of His essence, so it must be speech (kalam). The argument then examines the three possible loci for originated speech: in God's essence, in another, or not in any locus. Each is shown to be impossible, forcing the conclusion that speech is pre-eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's structure
Read the Arabic and translation for the second argument. Notice it presents a disjunction: either God speaks by essence or by speech. The first is rejected by consensus, so we focus on speech.
2
Understand the trichotomy for originated speech
If speech were originated (hadith), it would have to inhere in God's essence, in another being, or in no locus at all. These are the only possibilities.
This is a standard classification in kalam.
3
Examine each impossibility
Each option is ruled out: in God's essence would mean accidents in God (impossible); in another would allow attributing other deficits like ignorance to God; not in a locus is impossible for an attribute.
The argument uses analogical reasoning for the second case.
4
Conclude pre-eternity
Since all three are impossible, the speech cannot be originated; it must be pre-eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم الكلام بأشياء:
الأول: إنه تعالى حي، وكل حي فإنه يصح أن يكون متكلماً، وكل من صح عليه الاتصاف بصفة، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة أو بضدها. وضد الكلام هو الخرس والسكوت. وذلك نقص، والنقص على الله تعالى محال، فوجب أن يكون في الأزل موصوفاً بالكلام.
والثاني: وهو أنا أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم، فأما أن يكون متكلماً لذاته - وهو باطل بالاتفاق - أو يكون متكلماً بالكلام، وذلك الكلام إن كان حادثاً، فأما أن يحدث في ذاته، أو في غيره، أو لا في محل: والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فبطل كون الكلام حادثاً، فوجب أن يكون قديماً. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في ذات الله محال، لأنه يوجب قيام الحوادث بذات الله وهو محال، وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في غيره محال، لأنه لو جاز أن يكون متكلماً بكلام حاصل في غيره، لجاز أن يكون جاهلاً بجهل يقوم بغيره، وعاجزاً بعجز يقوم بغيره. وذلك باطل. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام لا في محل: محال. فبالاتفاق. وأيضاً: فكلام الله تعالى صفة، وصفة الشيء تكون حاصلة فيه لا محالة.

And if it is an attribute of deficiency, then it is necessary that He never be described with it, because deficiency upon God—mighty and majestic—is impossible. Commentary
This passage presents four rational arguments for the pre-eternity (qidam) of divine speech (kalam). The first argument is based on the principle that for any attribute that can be predicated, the subject must be described either by that attribute or its opposite. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: The argument does not assume the conclusion; it eliminates all possibilities for speech being originated, thus proving it must be pre-eternal. The assumption that God is speaking is a separate consensus, not the conclusion.

Checkpoint
Why is originated speech in another being considered impossible in the second argument?
A) Because it would deny God's uniqueness.
B) Because it would allow analogous imperfections like ignorance to be attributed to God.
C) Because speech cannot exist outside a body.
D) Because it would make God dependent on creation.

Key Takeaway
The second argument proves speech is pre-eternal by showing that all possible ways for it to be originated are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s speech is pre-eternal and directly from His essence, then every word of the Quran is a direct communication from Him to you. When you recite or hear the Quran, do you truly feel that urgency—that the Creator of the universe is speaking to you right now? Or have you become numb to that reality? What will you do today to restore that awe?
?
The argument shows that God’s attributes cannot be borrowed from creation. In your life, are there times when you attribute to God human-like qualities—such as changing His mind or reacting emotionally—without realizing it? How does this proof call you to purify your conception of God? Will you commit to studying His names and attributes more carefully?
?
If speech must inhere in the speaker, then God’s speech is inseparable from His essence. When you pray, do you listen for His response in the Quran? Or do you treat prayer as a monologue? What would change if you approached every verse as if God is personally addressing your current situation? Will you start reading the Quran with that expectation tonight?

Divine Attributes

Third Proof: Speech as Attribute of Perfection
Third rational proof using perfection-deficiency classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech, which classifies speech as either a perfection or a deficiency and concludes that since God has it, it must be a perfection and therefore possessed pre-eternally.

Core Idea
If speech were a deficiency, God would not have it; since He does, it is a perfection and must be eternal.

The third argument is based on the duality of attributes: every attribute is either a perfection (kamāl) or a deficiency (naqṣ). If speech is a perfection, God must always have it, because being without a perfection is a deficiency. If speech were a deficiency, God could not have it at all. Since we agree God has speech, it must be a perfection that He possesses in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's classification
Read the Arabic and translation for the third argument. Note the initial division: speech is either an attribute of perfection or deficiency.
2
Consider the first branch
If speech is perfection, then God must be described by it always (eternally). The absence of a perfection in God would be a deficiency.
This is a necessary implication.
3
Consider the second branch
If speech is deficiency, it cannot be attributed to God at all, because deficiency is impossible for Him.
4
Reach the conclusion
Since we agree God is described with speech (based on revelation and consensus), it cannot be a deficiency; it must be a perfection, and therefore God has it pre-eternally.
The argument relies on a previously established agreement that God speaks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم الكلام بأشياء:
الأول: إنه تعالى حي، وكل حي فإنه يصح أن يكون متكلماً، وكل من صح عليه الاتصاف بصفة، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة أو بضدها. وضد الكلام هو الخرس والسكوت. وذلك نقص، والنقص على الله تعالى محال، فوجب أن يكون في الأزل موصوفاً بالكلام.

Checkpoint
How does the third argument conclude that speech is an attribute of perfection?
A) By revelation directly stating it.
B) By eliminating the possibility of deficiency because God has speech.
C) By comparing speech to other attributes.

Key Takeaway
Since God possesses speech, it must be an attribute of perfection and therefore pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God's speech is a perfection and eternal. Do you treat His speech—the Quran—as eternal and perfect in your daily life, or do you approach it as just another book? What would change if you truly believed every word is from the Eternal?
?
If lacking a perfection is a deficiency for God, and God has no deficiency, then you too are meant to strive for perfections. What one perfection—such as truthfulness, patience, or prayer—are you neglecting, and why do you accept that deficiency in yourself when you know God created you to be free of it?

Fourth Proof: Comparative Excellence
Fourth rational proof using comparative excellence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth rational argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech, which uses the principle that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker, and if God were not speaking in pre-eternity, a human speaker would be more perfect than God—an impossibility.

Core Idea
A speaker is greater than a non-speaker; if God were not speaking eternally, a human would surpass Him.

The fourth argument is based on self-evident knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) that a speaker is more perfect and better than a non-speaker. Since every human is a speaker, if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, the human would have a perfection that God lacks, making the human more perfect than the pre-eternal God—an obvious absurdity. Therefore, God must be described with speech in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's premise
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth argument. The first premise is that we have necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker.
This is considered intuitive.
2
Apply the premise to humans
We are humans and we are speakers. So we possess the perfection of speech when we exist.
3
Apply to God in pre-eternity
If God were not a speaker in pre-eternity (i.e., before creation), then at that time He would lack speech while humans (who later exist) would have it. This would make the human more perfect than God at the moment of the human's existence.
4
Conclude the impossibility
That comparison is impossible (muḥāl). Therefore, God must have been a speaker in pre-eternity, meaning His speech is pre-eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم الكلام بأشياء:
الأول: إنه تعالى حي، وكل حي فإنه يصح أن يكون متكلماً، وكل من صح عليه الاتصاف بصفة، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة أو بضدها. وضد الكلام هو الخرس والسكوت. وذلك نقص، والنقص على الله تعالى محال، فوجب أن يكون في الأزل موصوفاً بالكلام.
والثاني: وهو أنا أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم، فأما أن يكون متكلماً لذاته - وهو باطل بالاتفاق - أو يكون متكلماً بالكلام، وذلك الكلام إن كان حادثاً، فأما أن يحدث في ذاته، أو في غيره، أو لا في محل: والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فبطل كون الكلام حادثاً، فوجب أن يكون قديماً. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في ذات الله محال، لأنه يوجب قيام الحوادث بذات الله وهو محال، وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام في غيره محال، لأنه لو جاز أن يكون متكلماً بكلام حاصل في غيره، لجاز أن يكون جاهلاً بجهل يقوم بغيره، وعاجزاً بعجز يقوم بغيره. وذلك باطل. وإنما قلنا: إن حدوث الكلام لا في محل: محال. فبالاتفاق. وأيضاً: فكلام الله تعالى صفة، وصفة الشيء تكون حاصلة فيه لا محالة.
والثالث: إن الكلام إما أن يكون صفة كمال، أو صفة نقص. فإن كان صفة كمال وجب أن يكون موصوفاً به أبداً. إذ لو لم يكن موصوفاً به في الأزل لزم كونه خالياً عن صفة الكمال، والخلو عن صفة الكمال نقصان، والنقصان على الله محال. وإذا كان صفة نقص وجب أن لا يتصف به البتة، لأن النقص على الله - عز وجل - محال. وحيث توافقنا على أنه تعالى قد اتصف به، علمنا أنه ليس من صفات النقص.
الرابع: إن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن المتكلم أكمل وأفضل ممن لم يكن متكلم، ولا شك أن الواحد منا متكلم. فلو لم يكن الله في الأزل متكلماً، لزم أن يكون حال الواحد منا عند وجوده أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى، حين كان في الأزل، وذلك محال. فيثبت أنه تعالى موصوف بالكلام في الأزل.
The proponents of the pre-eternity of speech argued with things: First: He, exalted, is living, and every living being is such that it is valid for it to be speaking, and everyone for whom description with an attribute is valid, then it must be described with that attribute or its opposite. The opposite of speech is muteness and silence. That is deficiency, and deficiency upon God, exalted, is impossible, so it is necessary that He be described with speech in pre-eternity. Second: That we have agreed that He, exalted, is speaking. Either He is speaking by His essence—which is invalid by consensus—or He is speaking by speech. That speech, if it were originated, then either it occurs in His essence, or in another, or not in a locus. The three divisions are invalid, so the occurrence of speech as originated is invalid, so it must be pre-eternal. We say: The occurrence of speech in the essence of God is impossible because it necessitates the occurrence of accidents in the essence of God, and that is impossible. The occurrence of speech in another is impossible because if it were permissible for Him to be speaking by speech subsisting in another, then it would be permissible for Him to be ignorant by ignorance subsisting in another, and powerless by powerlessness subsisting in another. That is invalid. The occurrence of speech not in a locus is impossible by consensus. Also, the speech of God, exalted, is an attribute, and the attribute of a thing occurs in it necessarily. Third: Speech is either an attribute of perfection or an attribute of deficiency. If it is an attribute of perfection, then it is necessary that He be described with it always. For if He were not described with it in pre-eternity, it would necessitate His being devoid of the attribute of perfection, and being devoid of the attribute of perfection is deficiency, and deficiency upon God is impossible. And if it is an attribute of deficiency, then it is necessary that He never be described with it, because deficiency upon God—mighty and majestic—is impossible. Since we have agreed that He, exalted, is described with it, we know that it is not from the attributes of deficiency. Fourth: Necessary knowledge exists that the speaker is more perfect and better than one who is not a speaker. There is no doubt that each one of us is a speaker. If God were not speaking in pre-eternity, then the state of each one of us at the time of his existence would be better and more perfect than God, exalted, when He was in pre-eternity, and that is impossible. So it is established that He, exalted, is described with speech in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents four rational arguments for the pre-eternity (qidam) of divine speech (kalam). The first argument is based on the principle that for any attribute that can be predicated, the subject must be described either by that attribute or its opposite. Since God is living, speaking is possible; its opposite (muteness/silence) is a deficiency, impossible for God, so He must be speaking in pre-eternity. The second argument uses a disjunctive syllogism: we agree God is speaking; either by essence (invalid by consensus) or by speech. That speech, if originated, must subsist in His essence, in another, or in no locus. All three are impossible: in His essence because it would entail accidents in God; in another because that would allow other deficiencies like ignorance to be attributed to God through an external substrate; without a locus by consensus. Also, an attribute must inhere in its subject. Therefore speech is pre-eternal. The third argument classifies speech as either perfection or deficiency. If perfection, God must always have it (absence of perfection is deficiency). If deficiency, He must never have it. Since He does have it, it is perfection, so He has it pre-eternally. The fourth argument uses comparative excellence: a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker. We are speakers; if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, we would be more perfect than the pre-eternal God, which is impossible.

Key Terms
كلام
علم ضروري
أفضل
أكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the self-evident truth used in the fourth argument?
A) God is the most perfect.
B) A speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker.
C) Speech is an attribute of perfection.
D) Humans are speakers.

Key Takeaway
If God lacked speech in pre-eternity, a human speaker would be more perfect than God, which is impossible; so God must have speech pre-eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not eternally speaking, you would be more perfect than Him at the moment you speak. Does that thought humble you or fill you with awe? How does this truth affect the way you value your own speech?
?
God is the eternal Speaker, yet you remain silent when you should speak for truth and justice. Where in your life are you choosing silence over speech, and what does that say about your reliance on God's perfect attribute?
?
The argument uses your own existence as a speaker to prove God's eternal speech. When you speak, are you conscious that your ability is a reflection of God's attribute, or do you take it for granted? What change will you make today to honor God's speech in your conversations?

Objection and Response to the First Argument
Presents the first objection and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector rejects the premise that silence is a deficiency and how addressing nonexistent beings is considered the real deficiency.

Core Idea
Silence is not a deficiency; addressing the nonexistent is.

The first objection targets the claim that silence would be a deficiency in God. The objector argues that true deficiency lies not in silence but in uttering commands to nonexistent beings. This lesson unpacks that objection and the example used to illustrate it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's target
The objection challenges the idea that God must be eternally speaking because silence would be a deficiency. Read the passage to see how the objector reframes deficiency.
2
Analyze the example
The objector gives an analogy of a man alone in a house addressing nonexistent people. Consider why this is considered foolish and how it applies to God.
The example is meant to show that addressing the nonexistent is irrational.
3
Connect to divine speech
The objector concludes that God's pre-eternal address to nonexistent beings would be similarly deficient, thus the first argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول: أما الوجه الأول: فقد تكلمنا عليه في مسألة السمع والبصر، والذي نريده ههنا هو أن نقول: لا نسلم أن السكوت نقص، بل النقص أن يقول القائل: يا زيد صل، ويا عمرو مم، مع أن زيداً وعمرواً يكونان معدومين، ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا جلس في دار نفسه وحده، خالياً عن الناس ثم يقول: يا مستقر اركب، ويا قائماً أقبل، فإن كل أحد يقضي عليه بالجنون والنقص، فكذا ههنا.
وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو أن قولكم: أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم فنقول: إن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً أنه فعل أفعالاً مخصوصة، دلت تلك الأفعال على كونه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأفعال، وكارهاً لبعضها، فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بشيء من الصفات المسماة بالكلام، وإن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً أمراً وراء ذلك فهذا ممنوع. والاتفاق ليس إلا في اللفظ.
وأما الوجه الثالث: وهو أن الكلام صفة كمال، والخلو عنه نقص، والنقص على الله محال. فجوابه: أن الاشتغال بالأمر والنهي حال عدم المأمور والمنهي، هو النقص والسفه. وهذا بعينه هو الجواب عن الرابع.
فهذا خلاصة الكلام المعقول في هذا الباب. وأما اللفظيات والسمعيات، ففيها أقوال كثيرة لا تليق بهذا الكتاب. والله أعلم بالصواب. ومنه التوفيق.
And a speaker may say: As for the first point: we have already discussed it in the issue of hearing and sight. What we intend here is to say: we do not concede that silence is a deficiency. Rather, deficiency is that the speaker says: 'O Zayd, pray,' and 'O Amr, go,' while Zayd and Amr are nonexistent. Do you not see that if a man sits in his house alone, empty of people, then says: 'O settled one, ride,' and 'O standing one, approach,' then everyone judges him to be insane and deficient. So likewise here. As for the second point: That is your statement: 'We have agreed that He, exalted, is speaking.' Then we say: If you mean by His being speaking that He performed specific actions, those actions indicating that He, exalted, is willing of some actions and disliking of others, then this is accepted, except that this extent does not indicate that He, exalted, is described with any of the attributes called speech. And if you mean by His being speaking something beyond that, then this is denied. The consensus is only on the word. As for the third point: That is that speech is an attribute of perfection, and being devoid of it is deficiency, and deficiency upon God is impossible. Its answer: That engagement in command and prohibition in the state of the absence of the commanded and the prohibited is deficiency and foolishness. This very thing is the answer to the fourth point. This is the summary of rational speech on this topic. As for verbal and revealed matters, there are many statements in them not befitting this book. And God knows best what is correct. And from Him is success.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the four arguments and their answers. The objector (qa'il) first addresses the first argument: he does not accept that silence is a deficiency. Rather, true deficiency is when one addresses nonexistent beings—like saying 'O Zayd, pray' while Zayd does not exist. This is considered foolishness (junun) and deficiency. If God addressed nonexistent beings in pre-eternity, that would be similar foolishness, so the first argument fails. The second objection challenges the consensus on God being 'speaking' (mutakallim). The objector argues that if by 'speaking' they mean He performed specific actions indicating His will, that is accepted but does not prove a subsisting attribute called speech. If they mean something else, it is denied; the consensus is only verbal. The third objection claims that speech is a perfection, so its absence would be deficiency. The answer: engaging in command and prohibition when the commanded and prohibited do not exist is itself deficiency and foolishness, not perfection. The same answer applies to the fourth argument. The passage concludes that this is the rational summary, and that verbal and revealed matters have many opinions not suitable for this book.

Key Terms
كلام
سكوت
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Silence is always a deficiency in a speaker.
Correction: The passage shows that true deficiency is addressing nonexistent beings, not silence itself.

Checkpoint
What does the objector identify as the real deficiency?
A) Silence
B) Addressing nonexistent beings
C) Lack of communication
D) Being alone

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that silence is not a deficiency; rather, addressing nonexistent beings is the true deficiency, and thus the argument for pre-eternal speech fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about something that doesn't exist—like spreading a rumor about someone's action that never happened, or worrying about a future that may never come? How does this lesson's definition of deficiency challenge you to ground your words and thoughts in reality?
?
When you find yourself silently anxious or fearful, do you consider that silence itself is not a deficiency? What would it take for you to trust God's silence in your life, knowing He is not deficient?
?
Think of a time you felt pressured to speak or act because silence seemed weak. How can this redefinition of true deficiency—addressing the nonexistent—free you to remain silent when words would be empty?

Objection to the Consensus Argument
Presents the second objection and response regarding the meaning of 'mutakallim'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's distinction between two meanings of 'mutakallim' and why the consensus is only verbal.

Core Idea
The consensus on God being 'speaking' is only about the word, not the attribute.

The second objection challenges the claim that consensus proves God has a subsisting attribute of speech. The objector argues that 'mutakallim' can be interpreted in two ways, and only one is accepted, which does not prove the desired attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
Read the passage and identify the two possible meanings of 'mutakallim' that the objector presents.
One meaning is about actions indicating will; the other is about a subsisting attribute.
2
See which is accepted
The objector accepts the first meaning (actions indicating will) but says it does not prove speech as an attribute.
3
Recognize the limitation of consensus
The objector concludes that the consensus is only on the word, not on the reality of the attribute.
This is a key point in theological debates about attributes.

?

Could your certainty in a particular interpretation be based on the word alone, like the consensus described in this lesson? Examine your heart: is your faith in God's speech rooted in what He has revealed, or in a human-made theological construct?
?

Are you willing to admit uncertainty or do you cling to labels?

Is it from rational arguments or from the direct experience of its impact on your soul? How would you answer someone who questions the basis of your belief? Let this question unsettle you until you find an answer that is truly your own.

Objection to the Perfection Argument and Ruling of Rational Speech
Presents the third and fourth objections with their shared response, and concludes the rational discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector considers commanding nonexistent beings as deficiency, and how the rational discussion on divine speech concludes.

Core Idea
Commanding the nonexistent is deficiency, not silence.

The third and fourth objections argue that speech is a perfection and its absence is a deficiency. The response, which also covers the fourth objection, is that engaging in command and prohibition when the commanded and prohibited do not exist is itself deficiency and foolishness. The lesson also includes the concluding remark that this is the summary of rational discourse, distinguishing it from verbal and revealed matters.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims speech is an attribute of perfection, so God must possess it eternally. Read the passage to see the response.
2
Analyze the response
The response argues that commanding nonexistent beings is deficiency and foolishness, so the absence of such command is not a deficiency.
This same answer applies to the fourth objection.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage states that this is the summary of rational speech, and that verbal/revealed matters have many opinions not suitable for the book.
This distinguishes rational theology from scriptural details.

ولقائل أن يقول: أما الوجه الأول: فقد تكلمنا عليه في مسألة السمع والبصر، والذي نريده ههنا هو أن نقول: لا نسلم أن السكوت نقص، بل النقص أن يقول القائل: يا زيد صل، ويا عمرو مم، مع أن زيداً وعمرواً يكونان معدومين، ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا جلس في دار نفسه وحده، خالياً عن الناس ثم يقول: يا مستقر اركب، ويا قائماً أقبل، فإن كل أحد يقضي عليه بالجنون والنقص، فكذا ههنا.
وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو أن قولكم: أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم فنقول: إن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً أنه فعل أفعالاً مخصوصة، دلت تلك الأفعال على كونه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأفعال، وكارهاً لبعضها، فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بشيء من الصفات المسماة بالكلام، وإن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً أمراً وراء ذلك فهذا ممنوع. والاتفاق ليس إلا في اللفظ.
وأما الوجه الثالث: وهو أن الكلام صفة كمال، والخلو عنه نقص، والنقص على الله محال. فجوابه: أن الاشتغال بالأمر والنهي حال عدم المأمور والمنهي، هو النقص والسفه. وهذا بعينه هو الجواب عن الرابع.
فهذا خلاصة الكلام المعقول في هذا الباب. وأما اللفظيات والسمعيات، ففيها أقوال كثيرة لا تليق بهذا الكتاب. والله أعلم بالصواب. ومنه التوفيق.
And a speaker may say: As for the first point: we have already discussed it in the issue of hearing and sight. What we intend here is to say: we do not concede that silence is a deficiency. Rather, deficiency is that the speaker says: 'O Zayd, pray,' and 'O Amr, go,' while Zayd and Amr are nonexistent. Do you not see that if a man sits in his house alone, empty of people, then says: 'O settled one, ride,' and 'O standing one, approach,' then everyone judges him to be insane and deficient. So likewise here. As for the second point: That is your statement: 'We have agreed that He, exalted, is speaking.' Then we say: If you mean by His being speaking that He performed specific actions, those actions indicating that He, exalted, is willing of some actions and disliking of others, then this is accepted, except that this extent does not indicate that He, exalted, is described with any of the attributes called speech. And if you mean by His being speaking something beyond that, then this is denied. The consensus is only on the word. As for the third point: That is that speech is an attribute of perfection, and being devoid of it is deficiency, and deficiency upon God is impossible. Its answer: That engagement in command and prohibition in the state of the absence of the commanded and the prohibited is deficiency and foolishness. This very thing is the answer to the fourth point. This is the summary of rational speech on this topic. As for verbal and revealed matters, there are many statements in them not befitting this book. And God knows best what is correct. And from Him is success.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the four arguments and their answers. The objector (qa'il) first addresses the first argument: he does not accept that silence is a deficiency. Rather, true deficiency is when one addresses nonexistent beings—like saying 'O Zayd, pray' while Zayd does not exist. This is considered foolishness (junun) and deficiency. If God addressed nonexistent beings in pre-eternity, that would be similar foolishness, so the first argument fails. The second objection challenges the consensus on God being 'speaking' (mutakallim). The objector argues that if by 'speaking' they mean He performed specific actions indicating His will, that is accepted but does not prove a subsisting attribute called speech. If they mean something else, it is denied; the consensus is only verbal. The third objection claims that speech is a perfection, so its absence would be deficiency. The answer: engaging in command and prohibition when the commanded and prohibited do not exist is itself deficiency and foolishness, not perfection. The same answer applies to the fourth argument. The passage concludes that this is the rational summary, and that verbal and revealed matters have many opinions not suitable for this book.

Key Terms
كمال
نقص
أمر
نهي
لفظيات
سمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If speech is a perfection, God must eternally speak in the sense of commanding.
Correction: The response shows that commanding nonexistents is not perfection but deficiency, so God's silence regarding nonexistents is not a lack.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is considered deficiency and foolishness?
A) Silence
B) Commanding nonexistent beings
C) Attributing speech to God
D) Denying divine attributes

Key Takeaway
The third objection is answered by showing that commanding nonexistents is deficiency, and the same answer refutes the fourth. The rational discussion ends here, distinguishing it from scriptural details.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself commanding or demanding things from others without a real basis—perhaps out of pride or control—are you displaying deficiency? Reflect on a recent situation and resolve to change.
?
The passage distinguishes between rational truth and secondary verbal disputes. In your own study of faith, are you often distracted by debates over wording instead of focusing on core rational truths? What can you do to prioritize what is essential?
?
God's perfection means He does not engage in what would be deficiency. Is there any aspect of your life where you persist in a behavior that, upon reflection, is actually foolish or degrading? Will you cut it off today?

Fourth Rational Proof: God's Speech in Pre-Eternity
The author presents the fourth rational proof that God is described with speech in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God must be described with speech in pre-eternity based on a comparison of perfection.

Core Idea
If a human speaker is more perfect than a non-speaking God, then God must be speaker.

This lesson presents the fourth of several rational proofs for the eternity of God's attribute of speech. The argument uses a simple but powerful comparison: we instinctively know that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker. Since every human is a speaker, if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, a human would surpass God—which is impossible. Let's examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the logical flow: premise, conditional, absurd consequence, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the claim that 'the speaker is more complete and excellent than the non-speaker'. This is presented as necessary knowledge.
This premise is taken as self-evident.
3
Follow the conditional logic
The argument says: if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, then a human being (who is a speaker) would be better than God at that time. That is impossible, so the antecedent must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is described with speech in pre-eternity. Consider why this matters for Islamic theology.
This establishes that speech is an eternal attribute of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن المتكلم أكمل وأفضل ممن لم يكن متكلم ، ولا شك أن الواحد منا متكلم . فلو لم يكن الله في الأزل متكلماً ، لزم أن يكون حال الواحد منا عند وجوده أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى ، حين كان في الأزل ، وذلك محال . فيثبت أنه تعالى موصوف بالكلام في الأزل .
The fourth: Necessary knowledge is obtained that the speaker is more complete and more excellent than one who is not a speaker, and there is no doubt that each one of us is a speaker. So if God were not a speaker in pre-eternity, it would necessarily follow that the state of each one of us at the time of his existence is better and more complete than God the Exalted, when He was in pre-eternity, and that is impossible. So it is established that He the Exalted is described with speech in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth rational argument for God's attribute of speech (kalam) being eternal. The argument is based on a comparison of perfection: we know by necessary knowledge that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker. Since every human is a speaker at the time of his existence, if God were not a speaker before creating humans, then at that pre-eternal moment a human (when later existing) would be more perfect than God—since the human would be a speaker while God would not. This is absurd, so God must have been a speaker in pre-eternity. The argument relies on the principle that God cannot be less perfect than a creature in any respect.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
المتكلم
الكلام
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument claims humans are actually better than God.
Correction: The argument only shows a logical absurdity if God were not a speaker; it does not assert actual superiority.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise used in this argument?
A) All humans are speakers.
B) A speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker.
C) God must be like humans.
D) Silence is a deficiency.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute, proven by a rational reductio argument comparing perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal speech is the Qur'an, do you revere the Qur'an as the very word of the eternal Speaker? When you recite it, are you aware that you are uttering something that has always been with Him? If not, what does that reveal about your connection to His attribute of speech?
?
The argument shows that denying God's eternal speech would absurdly make humans more perfect than Him. Yet, in your daily life, do you ever prioritize your own speech over listening to God's speech? When was the last time you truly paused to listen to His words instead of rushing to speak?
?
If you truly believed that God is the eternal Speaker, would you approach the Qur'an as a personal address from the Creator? How would that change the way you read it, feel about it, and respond to it starting today?
