Objection to the First Argument: Silence Is Not Deficiency When No One to Address
The objector argues that silence is not a deficiency when there is no one to address.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that silence is not inherently a deficiency, especially when there is no listener to address.

Core Idea
Silence is not deficiency; deficiency is commanding non-existents.

The previous argument claimed that God's silence before creation would be a deficiency. But the objector pushes back, distinguishing between silence itself and the act of addressing nonexistent beings. This passage presents that objection through a vivid analogy, showing that true deficiency lies in speaking to no one.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the objector structures his argument: first stating his position, then giving an analogy.
The analogy of the lone man is central to understanding the objection.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
Find the sentence where the objector says 'we do not concede that silence is a deficiency.' Then locate the redefinition of deficiency.
The objector does not deny that some silence could be deficiency; he narrows the definition.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy of a man alone in his house addressing imaginary people. Ask: Why would that be considered insane? How does it apply to the original argument about God?
The analogy shows that speaking to non-existent addressees is irrational, not silence itself.
4
Connect to the original argument
Reflect on how the objection refutes the claim that God's pre-creational silence is a deficiency. The objector implies that God's silence was appropriate because there were no existing beings to address.
This objection does not deny God's speech; it contextualizes when speech is meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : أما الوجه الأول : فقد تكلمنا عليه في مسألة السمع والبصر ، والذي نريده ههنا هو أن نقول : لا نسلم أن السكوت نقص ، بل النقص أن يقول القائل : يا زيد صل، ويا عمرو مم . مع أن زيداً وعمرواً يكونان معدومين ، ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا جلس في دار نفسه وحده ، خالياً عن الناس ثم يقول : يا مستقر اركب ، ويا قائماً أقبل . فإن كل أحد يقضي عليه بالجنون والنقص ، فكذا ههنا .
And one may say: As for the first aspect: we have already discussed it in the question of hearing and sight. What we intend here is to say: we do not concede that silence is a deficiency. Rather, the deficiency is that someone says: 'O Zayd, pray! and O Amr, die!' while Zayd and Amr are non-existent. Do you not see that a man, if he sits alone in his own house, secluded from people, and then says: 'O stationary one, ride! and O standing one, come forward!' – then everyone would judge him to be insane and deficient. So likewise here.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an objection to an earlier argument (the first argument, not reproduced here) that claimed silence is a deficiency. The objector refutes this by distinguishing between silence in general and silence in specific contexts. He argues that silence is not inherently a deficiency; rather, the deficiency lies in uttering commands and prohibitions to non-existent beings. He gives an analogy: a person alone in his house who addresses imaginary people ('O stationary one, ride!') would be considered insane. Similarly, if God had spoken commands before creating the addressees, that would be foolish. Thus, the objector denies that God's pre-eternal silence (i.e., not yet speaking) is a deficiency, because there was no one to address.

Correction: The objection does not deny God's speech; it rejects the idea that silence is always a deficiency, especially when there is no one to address.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, when is silence not a deficiency?
A) When the speaker is God.
B) When there is no one to address.
C) When the speaker is alone.
D) When the speech is about prayer.

Key Takeaway
Silence is not a deficiency when there is no one to address; deficiency consists in commanding non-existent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there situations where you continue to 'command' or speak to people, possibilities, or outcomes that do not exist, hoping they will appear? How can you stop this habit and instead wait patiently like the man who sits alone without speaking?
?
When you find yourself filling silence with empty words just to avoid awkwardness, what does that reveal about your own need for validation? How can you practice silence as a strength, trusting that God's silence is not a deficiency?

Objection to Consensus: Two Meanings of 'Mutakallim'
Objector distinguishes two meanings of 'mutakallim'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector refutes the consensus argument by distinguishing between God being mutakallim in terms of actions and in terms of a real attribute.

Core Idea
Consensus on the word 'mutakallim' does not prove a real attribute of speech.

The second argument for God being mutakallim relied on consensus. The objector challenges this by showing that the word 'mutakallim' can be interpreted in two different ways. Let's examine this objection closely to see how it undermines the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two conditional statements: 'if you mean... then...'.
The objector uses a conditional structure to separate the two possible meanings.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two interpretations of 'mutakallim' that the objector considers. One is conceded, the other is denied.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction show that the consensus is only verbal? How does it weaken the original argument?
The original argument claimed consensus on God being mutakallim, but here the objector shows the consensus does not refer to a single, agreed-upon meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو أن قولكم : أجمعنا على أنه تعالى متكلم فنقول : إن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً أنه فعل أفعالاً مخصوصة ، دلت تلك الأفعال على كونه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأفعال، وكارهاً لبعضها ، فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بشيء من الصفات المسماة بالكلام ، وإن عنيتم بكونه متكلماً : أمراً وراء ذلك فهذا ممنوع . والاتفاق ليس إلا في اللفظ
And as for the second aspect: which is your saying: 'We have consensus that He the Exalted is a speaker (mutakallim).' So we say: If you mean by His being a speaker that He did specific actions, those actions indicate that He the Exalted is willing of some actions and disliking of others, then this is conceded, except that this amount does not indicate that He the Exalted is described with any of the attributes called speech. And if you mean by His being a speaker something beyond that, then this is denied. The consensus is only in the wording.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an objection to the second argument (which claimed consensus that God is mutakallim). The objector distinguishes between two interpretations of 'mutakallim': (1) as someone who performs specific actions (like creating) that indicate will or dislike—this is granted but does not prove an inherent attribute of speech; (2) as someone who has a real attribute of speech beyond actions—this is denied. Thus, the consensus is only about the word 'mutakallim', not about the reality of an attribute. The objector undermines the evidential value of consensus by showing it is ambiguous.

Correction: The objector shows that the consensus is only about the word, not about a specific attribute. The term 'mutakallim' can be interpreted as referring to actions, which does not imply a real attribute.

Checkpoint
What does the objector concede about God being mutakallim?
A) That God has a real attribute of speech.
B) That God performed specific actions indicating will.
C) That God speaks letters and sounds.
D) That consensus is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The consensus on God being 'mutakallim' is only about the word, not about a specific attribute of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
In your own conversations about God, do you ever insist on a particular theological definition simply because it's what everyone says? What would it cost you to admit that some meanings are merely 'in wording' and not necessarily about reality?

Objection: Command Without Subjects is Foolishness
The objector argues that commanding non-existent beings is foolishness, answering arguments three and four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector refutes the argument that God must have speech in pre-eternity by showing that commanding non-existent beings is foolishness.

Core Idea
Speech is only perfect when directed to existing addressees.

The third argument claimed that speech is a perfection and its absence a deficiency, so God must have speech eternally. The objector counters that commanding or prohibiting non-existent beings is not perfection but foolishness. This same reasoning also answers the fourth argument about the superiority of the speaker. In this lesson, we examine this objection and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice it restates the third argument and then gives a single answer that applies to both the third and fourth arguments.
The objector accepts that speech is generally a perfection, but reframes the specific context.
2
Understand the logical twist
The objector agrees that speech is a perfection in general, but argues that commanding or prohibiting non-existent beings is actually a deficiency (foolishness). Thus, God's silence before creation was not a lack of perfection but the appropriate state.
This turns the original argument on its head.
3
Connect to broader implications
Reflect on how this objection undermines the claim that God must have speech eternally. It shows that attributes are only praiseworthy when exercised in appropriate circumstances.
Consider how this relates to the Ash'ari position on the eternity of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثالث : وهو أن الكلام صفة كمال ، والخلو عنه نقص ، والنقص على الله محال . فجوابه : أن الاشتغال بالأمر والنهي حال عدم المأمور والمنهي ، هو النقص والسفه . وهذا بعينه هو الجواب عن الرابع :
And as for the third aspect: which is that speech is an attribute of perfection, and being devoid of it is deficiency, and deficiency upon God is impossible. So its answer is: that occupying oneself with command and prohibition at the time of the absence of the commanded and prohibited person is deficiency and foolishness. And this very same is the answer to the fourth:
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the objector's response to the third argument (that speech is a perfection and its absence a deficiency) and similarly to the fourth argument (that a speaker is more perfect). The objector turns the argument around: while speech is indeed a perfection, commanding or prohibiting non-existent beings is not perfection but foolishness. Therefore, God's silence in pre-eternity (when no addressees existed) was not a deficiency; rather, it would have been foolishness to speak then. The same reasoning applies to the fourth argument: the comparison only holds when speech is meaningful. Thus, the attribute of speech in its perfect sense only applies when there are existing addressees.

الأمر والنهي

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The objector shows that speech is only a perfection when directed to existing addressees; otherwise, commanding non-existent beings is foolishness.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject the claim that God's lack of speech in pre-eternity is a deficiency?
A) Because God is beyond attributes.
B) Because commanding non-existent beings is foolishness.
C) Because silence is always better.
D) Because speech is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's silence in pre-eternity was not a deficiency because commanding non-existent beings would be foolish, not perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage teaches that speech is only perfect when it serves a real purpose for existing beings. Does your own speech—especially about God—pass that test? What would change if you treated every word you speak about the Divine as an address to someone who is listening?

The Disagreement on Pre-eternity and Everlastingness
Author presents two doctrinal views on pre-eternity and everlastingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing positions on whether God's attributes of pre-eternity and everlastingness are identical to His essence or additional attributes.

Core Idea
Theologians dispute: are pre-eternity and everlastingness God's essence or distinct attributes?

This passage from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah introduces a fundamental theological disagreement. Before examining the proofs, we must first grasp the two opposing views: one that identifies the attributes with the essence, and another that treats them as separate properties. Understanding the stakes of this debate is essential for following the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the disagreement passage
Read the Arabic sentence presenting the two views. Note the parallel structure: 'بعض يقول... ومنهم يقول...'.
The first view is tawḥīd al-ṣifāt (attributes identical to essence).
2
Identify the two positions
The first group says pre-eternity and everlastingness are exactly the same as God's specific essence. The second says they are two attributes existing in God.
This is a classic debate in kalām: ʿayn al-dhāt vs. ṣifāt qāʾimah.
3
Reflect on why this matters
This difference leads to different understandings of God's simplicity and the relationship between His essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القدم والبقاء
عن الناس من يقول : إن كونه قديماً باقياً عين ذاته المخصوصة . ومنهم من يقول : هما صفتان قائمتان بذات الله تعالى
واحتج الأولون بوجوه :
الأول : إن المفهوم من كون الشيء قديماً معقول لنا ، والذات المخصوصة التي هي ذات الله تعالى من حيث إنها هي غير معلومة لنا فوجب التغاير . بيان الأول : أن القديم هو الذي لا أول لوجوده ، والباقي هو الذي يكون مستقر الوجود ، وهذا المفهوم معقول . وأما أن الذات المخصوصة ، التي هي ذات الله تعالى غير معقولة ، فلما ثبت تقريره فيما تقدم وإذا لاخت المقدمتان ، فالنتيجة لازمة
On pre-eternity and everlastingness: Some people say that His being pre-eternal and everlasting is identical to His specific essence. And some of them say that they are two attributes subsisting in the essence of God the Exalted. The first group argued with proofs: The first: the concept of a thing being pre-eternal is intelligible to us, while the specific essence which is the essence of God the Exalted, insofar as it is itself, is not known to us, so distinction is necessary. Explanation of the first: the pre-eternal is that which has no beginning to its existence, and the everlasting is that which has stable existence, and this concept is intelligible. As for the specific essence, which is the essence of God the Exalted, being unintelligible, since its determination was established previously, and if the two premises are established, then the conclusion is necessary.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents a theological disagreement and then gives the first proof for one side. It begins by stating the disagreement: some say that God's attributes of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa') are identical to His specific essence (dhat), while others say they are two additional attributes (sifat) subsisting in God. The first group (those who distinguish the attributes from the essence) adduce proofs. The first proof is a syllogism: (1) The concept of a thing being pre-eternal is intelligible to us; (2) God's specific essence is not intelligible to us; therefore pre-eternity and the essence are distinct. The author then explains: 'pre-eternal' means that which has no beginning to its existence, and 'everlasting' means that which has stable existence, and this meaning is understood. But God's essence, being beyond human comprehension, is not intelligible. Since these two premises are true, the conclusion—that pre-eternity and everlastingness are distinct from the essence—necessarily follows.

The First Proof for Distinguishing Pre-eternity and Everlastingness
Author gives the first syllogistic proof for distinguishing the attributes from the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the syllogistic proof that pre-eternity and everlastingness are distinct from God's essence based on intelligibility.

Core Idea
What we can understand must differ from what we cannot: pre-eternity vs. God's essence.

The first group—those who argue that pre-eternity and everlastingness are additional attributes—now present their first proof. It is a simple but powerful syllogism: we have a clear concept of 'pre-eternal', but we cannot grasp God's essence; therefore the two must be different. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and explanation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Then read the author's explanation of the terms.
The proof is structured as a syllogism.
2
Identify the two premises
Premise one: The concept of a thing being pre-eternal is intelligible. Premise two: God's specific essence is not intelligible.
'Intelligible' means we can form a clear concept of it.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since intelligible and unintelligible are different, pre-eternity (and everlastingness) must be distinct from the essence.
The text says: 'وجب التغاير' (distinction is necessary).
4
Review the definitions
The author clarifies that 'pre-eternal' means having no beginning, and 'everlasting' means having stable existence. Both are clear concepts.
These definitions are crucial for the premise.

Two Views and Three Proofs for Qidam and Baqa'
The author presents the two views and three proofs for the identity of qidam and baqa' with the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether God's attributes of pre-eternity and permanence are identical with His essence or additional, and you will be able to explain the three proofs used by those who advocate identity.

Core Idea
The concept of God's pre-eternity is intelligible, but His essence is not – so they must be distinct in meaning.

This passage introduces two opposing theological positions: that God's attributes of pre-eternity and permanence are either identical with His essence or are additional attributes. The text then provides three argumentative proofs for the identity position. As you read, note how each proof uses a different logical approach to demonstrate a conceptual distinction between the essence and these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the opening lines that mention the two groups: those who say qidam and baqa' are identical with the essence, and those who say they are two attributes subsisting in the essence.
Notice the author labels the first group as 'al-awwalun'.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that the concept of pre-eternity is intelligible (ma'qul) while the specific essence is unknown to us (ghayr ma'luma), so they must be different.
This proof relies on the premise that we can understand 'pre-eternal' but cannot grasp God's essence.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof divides the pre-eternal permanent into four categories: body, substance, accident, or something else. Since the concept applies to all, it must be distinct from each category.
This is a 'division argument' showing that the attribute is a common concept.
4
Examine the third proof
The third proof contrasts two propositions: 'God's essence is pre-eternal and permanent' and 'God's essence is essence'. The first is informative, the second is tautological, indicating that the predicate is not identical to the subject.
If they were identical, the first proposition would be as uninformative as the second.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that these three proofs demonstrate that qidam and baqa' are attributes (sifah), i.e., distinct from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القدم والبقاء
عن الناس (١) من يقول : إن كونه قديماً باقياً عين ذاته المخصوصة . ومنهم من يقول : هما صفتان قائمتان بذات الله تعالى
واحتج الأولون بوجوه :
.
الأول : إن المفهوم من كون الشيء قديماً معقول لنا ، والذات المخصوصة التي هي ذات الله تعالى من حيث إنها هي غير ) معلومة لنا ) (٢) فوجب التغاير . بيان الأول : أن القديم هو الذي لا أول لوجوده ، والباقي هـو الذي يكون مستقر الوجود ، وهذا المفهوم معقول . وأما أن الذات المخصوصة ، التي هي ذات الله تعالى غير معقولة ، فلما ثبت تقريره فيما تقدم وإذا لاخت المقدمتان ، فالنتيجة لازمة
·
الثاني : إنا نقول القديم الباقي إما أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً ، أو شيئاً ما ( مغايراً ) (٣) لهذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، فنجعل المفهوم من القديم الباقي مورد التقسيم إلى هذه الأقسام الأربعة ، ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين الأقسام فالمفهوم من القديم الباقي ، أمر مشترك بين هذه الأقسام ، فوجب كونه مغايراً لكل هذه الأقسام .
الثالث : إنا نقول : إن ذات الله تعالى قديمة باقية ، فتكون القضية مفهومة . وإذا قلنا : إن ذات الله ( تعالى ذات )) لم يفد هذا الكلام شيئاً ، ولولا التغاير بين الذات وبين كونها باقية ، وإلا لقام كل واحد من هاتين القضيتين مقام الأخرى .
فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة ، دالة على أن كونه تعالى قديماً باقياً ، صفة
On pre-eternity and permanence. Among people are those who say that His being pre-eternal and permanent is identical to His specific essence. And among them are those who say that they are two attributes subsisting in the essence of God Most High. The first group argued with proofs: First: The concept of a thing being pre-eternal is intelligible to us, but the specific essence which is the essence of God Most High is not known to us, so there must be difference (taghayyur). Explanation of the first: That the pre-eternal is that which has no beginning to its existence, and the permanent is that which has stable existence, and this concept is intelligible. As for the specific essence which is the essence of God Most High being unintelligible, that has been established by its proof earlier. So when the two premises are established, the conclusion follows. Second: We say: the pre-eternal permanent is either a body, or a substance, or an accident, or something different from these three divisions. So we make the concept of the pre-eternal permanent the subject of division into these four divisions, and the subject of division is common among the divisions. So the concept of the pre-eternal permanent is something common among these divisions, so it must be different from all these divisions. Third: We say: The essence of God Most High is pre-eternal and permanent, so the proposition is understood. And if we say: 'the essence of God Most High is essence', this saying conveys nothing; and had there been no difference between the essence and its being permanent, then each of these two propositions would stand in place of the other. Thus these three proofs indicate that His being pre-eternal and permanent is an attribute.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether God's attributes of pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqa') are identical with His essence or are additional attributes. The author first states the two opposing views: some say they are identical with the essence, others say they are two attributes subsisting in the essence. Then the author gives three proofs for the first view (identity). The first proof argues that the concept of 'pre-eternal' is intelligible to us, but God's essence is not, so they must be different. The second proof uses a division: anything pre-eternal and permanent must fall into one of four categories (body, substance, accident, or something else); the concept is common to all, so it is distinct from each. The third proof shows that saying 'God's essence is pre-eternal and permanent' is informative, while 'God's essence is essence' is tautological, so the predicate is not the same as the subject. The author concludes that these three proofs demonstrate that qidam and baqa' are attributes (i.e., distinct from the essence). The style is argumentative and proceeds in numbered points.

Key Terms
qidam (قدم)
baqa' (بقاء)
dhat (ذات)
sifah (صفة)
taghayyur (تغاير)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some may think that 'identity' means the attributes are exactly the same as the essence without any conceptual distinction.
Correction: The passage argues for conceptual difference (taghayyur) while still maintaining real identity. The attributes are not additional to the essence in reality, but our understanding distinguishes them.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The three proofs demonstrate that the attributes of pre-eternity and permanence are conceptually distinct from God's essence, even though they may be identical in reality.

Two Proofs for Baqa' as an Additional Attribute
Two arguments for baqa' as additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used to support baqa' as an additional attribute and the objection to the first argument.

Core Idea
If baqa' were essential, God would not need it; if additional, infinite regress threatens.

In this passage, the author presents two arguments from those who affirm that permanence (baqa') is an attribute superadded to God's essence. The first argument is that if baqa' were an attribute subsisting in the essence, the essence would depend on it, compromising God's self-sufficiency. The second argument warns of an infinite regress if duration itself requires duration. We'll examine these arguments and a subtle objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the structure: the first proof includes an objection, and the second proof starts but ends abruptly.
The text is incomplete due to a page break.
2
Identify the two proofs and the objection
Notice that the first proof is followed by a potential objection ('one could say...'). The second proof begins with 'Second: If duration...' but cuts off mid-argument.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why the objection matters: it shifts the dependency from an external attribute to the essence itself. Also think about how infinite regress (tasalsul) is used as a reductio in the second proof.
Even though the second proof is incomplete, the regress argument is a classic kalām move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المتكرون لكون البقاء صفة زائدة على الذات بوجهين : الأول : إنه لو كان البقاء صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، لافتقرت الذات إلى تلك الصفة فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود لغيره ، وذلك محال . ولقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الذات المخصوصة لكونها تلك الذات المخصوصة توجب ) الدوام والاستمرار . والمحال إنما يلزم لو أثبتنا شيئاً وراء الذات يوجب ((٢) دوام تلك الذات . أما إن قلنا : الذات المخصوصة توجب ذلك الدوام ، لم يلزم منه محال . مع أن على هذا التقدير يكون ذلك الدوام صفة من صفات الذات
الثاني : إنه لو كان الدوام والاستمرار صفة ، لكانت تلك الصفة أيضاً دائمة مستمرة ، فيلزم أن يكون دوامها زائداً عليها ، ولزم التسلسل ، وأيضاً : فنقول : دوام تلك الصفة إما أن يكون لنفسها ، أو لشيء آخر ، فإن كان دوامها لنفسها ، وذوام تلك الذات لأجل تلك الصفة ، ( فحينئذ تكون تلك الصفة واجبة لنفسها ، وتكون الذات واجبة لأجل تلك الصفة ((٣) وما يكون دائماً لنفسه يكون أولى بكونه ذاتاً مستقلة بنفسها ، مما يكون دوامه بسبب غيره ،
And those who affirm (al-muthbitūn) argued that permanence is an attribute superadded to the essence in two ways: First: If permanence were an attribute subsisting in the essence of God Most High, then the essence would be in need of that attribute, and so He would be necessary of existence in Himself and necessary of existence through another, and that is impossible. But one could say: Why is it not permissible to say that the specific essence, by virtue of being that specific essence, necessitates duration and continuity? The impossible would only follow if we posit something beyond the essence that necessitates the duration of that essence. However, if we say that the specific essence necessitates that duration, no impossibility follows. Moreover, on this assumption, that duration would be an attribute of the essence. Second: If duration and continuity were an attribute, then that attribute would also be enduring and continuous, so its duration would have to be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. Also, we say: The duration of that attribute is either due to itself or to something else. If its duration is due to itself, and the duration of the essence is due to that attribute, then that attribute would be necessary in itself, and the essence would be necessary because of that attribute. And what is eternal in itself is more deserving of being an independent essence than that whose duration is due to another.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the group that affirms that permanence (baqa') is an additional attribute superadded to God's essence. The first argument is that if baqa' were an attribute subsisting in the essence, then the essence would depend on it, making God necessary through another in addition to being necessary in Himself, which is impossible. The author then includes a potential objection: one might say the essence itself necessitates duration by its very nature, not by a separate attribute. The response is that if we say the essence necessitates it, then the duration is still an attribute of the essence, not something separate, so the objection fails. The second argument is that if duration were an attribute, it would itself be enduring, requiring another attribute for its permanence, leading to infinite regress. Also, if the attribute's duration is self-caused and the essence's duration is caused by it, then the attribute would be more deserving of being an independent essence. The passage ends abruptly in the middle of the argument due to page break.

Key Terms
baqa' (بقاء)
sifah (صفة)
zā'id (زائد)
dhat (ذات)
wājib al-wujūd (واجب الوجود)
tasalsul (تسلسل)
dawām (دوام)
istimrār (استمرار)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof is thought to show that any attribute makes God dependent.
Correction: The objection clarifies that if the essence itself necessitates the attribute, no dependence on another arises; the problem is only if the attribute is external.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem raised in the first proof against baqa' being an additional attribute?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of attributes.
B) It would make God necessary of existence through another.
C) It implies that God's essence is composed of parts.
D) It contradicts divine simplicity.

Key Takeaway
The first proof fails if the essence itself necessitates duration, but the second proof starts an infinite regress argument that remains incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection to the first proof suggests that God's essence itself necessitates duration. Do you live as though God's permanence is an external 'thing' He possesses, or as an expression of who He is inherently? How does this affect your reliance on Him when everything around you is transient?
?
The second proof warns that if an attribute's duration is self-caused, that attribute becomes more deserving of being an independent essence than God. In your heart, are there any 'attributes' of God—like mercy, power, or justice—that you treat as almost separate beings, giving them a kind of independence in your mind? How can you realign your understanding to see them as inseparable from His one essence?
?
This logical argument can feel abstract. But if God's permanence were not intrinsic, He would depend on something else. Where in your life do you still depend on created things as sources of stability? How does the reminder of God's self-sustaining permanence (baqā') call you to transfer that trust from the temporal to the Eternal?

Three Proofs that Eternity and Permanence are Additional Attributes
The author presents three proofs that God's eternity and permanence are attributes added to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs establishing that God's eternity and permanence are attributes distinct from His essence.

Core Idea
Eternity and permanence are not synonyms for essence—they add meaning.

In this passage, the author presents three proofs (wujuh) demonstrating that God's being eternal (qadim) and permanent (baqi) are attributes additional to His essence (dhat). The third proof is articulated clearly: if 'eternal and permanent' were identical to essence, then saying 'essence is essence' would be equivalent to 'essence is eternal and permanent', which it is not. This logical distinction shows they are separate attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through before interpreting.
The passage references three proofs but only elaborates the third.
2
Identify the three proofs
Note that the author says 'فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة' (these three aspects) but only the third proof is fully stated. The first two are implied from the context of the source.
The third proof is the main focus of this excerpt.
3
Analyze the third proof
Examine the third proof: 'إن ذات الله تعالى قديمة باقية' gives a meaningful proposition, but 'إن ذات الله تعالى ذات' is meaningless. This shows 'qadim baqi' adds meaning to the essence.
The argument relies on the non-interchangeability of propositions.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that these proofs demonstrate that God's being eternal and permanent is an attribute additional to the essence.
This establishes the distinct reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمفهوم من القديم الباقي ، أمر مشترك بين هذه الأقسام ، فوجب كونه مغايراً لكل هذه الأقسام . الثالث : إنا نقول : إن ذات الله تعالى قديمة باقية ، فتكون القضية مفهومة . وإذا قلنا : إن ذات الله ( تعالى ذات ) لم يفد هذا الكلام شيئاً ، ولولا التغاير بين الذات وبين كونها باقية ، وإلا لقام كل واحد من هاتين القضيتين مقام الأخرى . فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة ، دالة على أن كونه تعالى قديماً باقياً ، صفة زائدة على الذات
The concept of 'the eternal, the permanent' is a matter common among these categories, so it must be distinct from each of these categories. Third: we say: The essence of God most high is eternal and permanent, so the proposition is understood. And if we say: 'The essence of God (most high essence)', this statement does not convey anything; and were it not for the distinction between the essence and its being permanent, then each of these two propositions would stand in the place of the other. So these three aspects indicate that His being eternal and permanent is an attribute additional to the essence.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) that God's being 'eternal' (qadim) and 'permanent' (baqi) is an attribute (sifa) additional to the essence (dhat). The author first notes that the concept of 'eternal, permanent' is common across different categories (aqsam) of existents, so it must be distinct from each category – implying it is a separate attribute. Then he gives the third proof: when we say 'the essence of God is eternal and permanent,' we have an intelligible proposition. If we try to replace 'eternal, permanent' with 'essence' alone (as if essence itself were the meaning), the statement becomes meaningless. This shows that the attribute 'eternal, permanent' is distinct from the essence, otherwise the two propositions would be interchangeable. The author concludes that these three aspects together prove that eternity and permanence are attributes added to the essence. Note: The first two proofs are not explicitly preserved in this excerpt but are referenced by the conclusion.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
باقي (baqi)
ذات (dhat)
صفة (sifa)
مغاير (mughayir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'eternal' and 'permanent' are synonymous with the essence itself.
Correction: The passage shows they are additional attributes because the proposition 'essence is eternal' is meaningful, whereas 'essence is essence' is not, proving they add meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why can't 'the essence of God is eternal and permanent' be replaced by 'the essence of God is essence'?
A) Because essence and attributes are completely separate entities.
B) Because the second statement would be meaningless, proving that 'eternal and permanent' adds meaning to the essence.
C) Because the first statement is a tautology.
D) Because attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity and permanence are attributes distinct from His essence, as shown by the logical non-interchangeability of propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is eternal,' do you truly feel the weight of that phrase as a distinct and meaningful attribute, or has it become a hollow repetition? How would your worship change if you lived in the reality of that attribute every moment?
?
If God's eternity and permanence are additional attributes that provide real knowledge of Him, then ignoring them means you know Him less. What specific step will you take this week to meditate on one of God's attributes as a distinct reality, not just a label?
?
The proof shows that simply saying 'God exists' conveys nothing compared to saying 'God is eternal.' In your own life, are you satisfied with a vague belief in God’s existence, or are you actively seeking to know His attributes as distinct, life-changing truths? What will you sacrifice to gain that deeper knowledge?

Two Objections to Baqa' as an Additional Attribute and Their Refutation
Two objections against baqa' as additional attribute and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against baqa' being an additional attribute and how they are refuted within the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
Baqa' as an attribute does not imply dependence if the essence itself necessitates it.

In this passage, the author presents two rational objections raised by those who deny that baqa' (permanence) is an attribute additional to God's essence. Each objection is followed by a refutation that shows the reasoning is flawed. By engaging with these objections, we clarify the relationship between God's essence and His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first objection and refutation
Notice how Objection 1 argues that if baqa' is an attribute, God would be dependent on it, causing a contradiction. Then see how the refutation offers an alternative: the essence itself necessitates permanence.
The refutation does not deny that baqa' is an attribute; it reinterprets its basis.
3
Identify the second objection and refutation
Objection 2 claims that if baqa' is an attribute, it too must be permanent, leading to infinite regress. The refutation examines two possibilities (self-causation or external causation) and shows both lead to impossibility.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Each objection is answered by showing an alternative possibility or an undesirable consequence. Consider how this strengthens the position that baqa' is an additional attribute.
The author concludes that the discourse is summarized, implying these are the main points.
5
Connect to divine attributes
Understand why this matters: clarifying the nature of baqa' helps define how God's attributes relate to His essence without compromise to His oneness.
This is a key issue in Ilm al-Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المتكرون لكون البقاء صفة زائدة على الذات بوجهين : الأول : إنه لو كان البقاء صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، لافتقرت الذات إلى تلك الصفة فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود لغيره ، وذلك محال . ولقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الذات المخصوصة لكونها تلك الذات المخصوصة توجب الدوام والاستمرار . والمحال إنما يلزم لو أثبتنا شيئاً وراء الذات يوجب دوام تلك الذات . أما إن قلنا : الذات المخصوصة توجب ذلك الدوام ، لم يلزم منه محال . مع أن على هذا التقدير يكون ذلك الدوام صفة من صفات الذات . الثاني : إنه لو كان الدوام والاستمرار صفة ، لكانت تلك الصفة أيضاً دائمة مستمرة ، فيلزم أن يكون دوامها زائداً عليها ، ولزم التسلسل ، وأيضاً : فنقول : دوام تلك الصفة إما أن يكون لنفسها ، أو لشيء آخر ، فإن كان دوامها لنفسها ، وذوام تلك الذات لأجل تلك الصفة ، فحينئذ تكون تلك الصفة واجبة لنفسها ، وتكون الذات واجبة لأجل تلك الصفة وما يكون دائماً لنفسه يكون أولى بكونه ذاتاً مستقلة بنفسها ، مما يكون دوامه بسبب غيره ، وإما إن كان دوام تلك الصفة بدوام شيء آخر ، فذلك الآخر إن كان هو الذات لزم الدور ، وإن كان شيئاً آخر لزم التسلسل ، والكل محال . فهذا حاصل الكلام المعقول الملخص في هذا الباب . والله أعلم
And those who deny [that baqa' is an additional attribute] argued for the fact that baqa' is an attribute additional to the essence with two points: The first: If baqa' were an attribute subsisting in God's essence, then the essence would be in need of that attribute, and so it would be necessary of existence by itself and necessary of existence by another, and that is impossible. But one may say: Why is it not permissible to say: the particular essence, because it is that particular essence, necessitates permanence and continuance? The impossible only follows if we posit something beyond the essence that necessitates the permanence of that essence. As for if we say: the particular essence necessitates that permanence, no impossibility follows from it. Moreover, on this assumption, that permanence is an attribute of the attributes of the essence. The second: If permanence and continuance were an attribute, then that attribute would also be permanent and continuant, so its permanence would have to be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. Also, we say: The permanence of that attribute is either by itself or by something else. If its permanence is by itself, and the permanence of the essence is by that attribute, then that attribute would be necessary by itself, and the essence would be necessary by that attribute; and what is permanent by itself is more deserving of being an independent essence than that whose permanence is due to another. And if the permanence of that attribute is by the permanence of something else, then if that something else is the essence, circularity follows; and if it is something else, infinite regress follows. All of that is impossible. So this is the gist of the rational speech summarized in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (wajhan) raised by those who deny (al-mutakirrun) that baqa' (permanence) is an additional attribute, along with their refutations. The first objection argues that if baqa' were an attribute subsisting in God's essence, then the essence would depend on it, causing a contradiction: God would be necessary by essence and also necessary by another. The refutation responds: it is permissible to say that the particular essence itself necessitates permanence, not some external thing. In that case, no impossibility arises, and that permanence is still an attribute of the essence. The second objection raises a regress: if baqa' is an attribute, then that attribute itself is permanent, requiring another attribute of permanence for it, ad infinitum. The refutation examines two possibilities: (1) the permanence of the attribute is by itself – then the attribute would be self-necessary and more deserving of being an independent essence than God, which is absurd; (2) its permanence is by something else – that something must be either the essence (circular) or something else (regress). Both are impossible. The author concludes that this summarizes the rational discourse on this topic.

Key Terms
بقاء
صفة زائدة
ذات
وجوب الوجود
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any additional attribute necessarily implies dependence on that attribute.
Correction: If the essence itself necessitates the attribute, no dependence follows; the attribute is still additional but not separate in need.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the second objection avoid the infinite regress initiated by the objection?
A) It proves that the attribute's permanence is due to itself, making the attribute self-sufficient.
B) It proves that the attribute's permanence is due to the essence, breaking the regress.
C) It shows that both possibilities (self-causation and external causation) lead to impossibility, thus the objection's premise is false.
D) It denies that the attribute is permanent.

Key Takeaway
The two objections against baqa' as an additional attribute are refuted by showing that the essence itself can necessitate permanence without circularity or regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutation shows that Allah's essence itself necessitates permanence—no external cause required. Think of one fear or insecurity you hold onto. Is that fear rooted in the belief that something other than Allah must give you stability? How does Allah’s self-necessitated permanence undercut that fear? What will you do this week to transfer your trust fully to Him?
?
The second objection leads to absurdities if you try to attribute permanence to the attribute separately. In your life, do you treat some of Allah's blessings as if they are independent and self-sustaining? Where have you fallen into the error of 'infinite regress' by trusting in chains of worldly causes instead of the Self-Sufficient? Identify one chain you rely on and consciously break it by turning directly to Allah.
?
The attribute of permanence is 'additional' yet not separate from the essence—a mystery that demands humility. Where in your understanding of Allah have you become too comfortable, assuming you’ve fully grasped His nature? How does this passage call you to submit to the limits of human reason? Commit to studying more about Allah's attributes while acknowledging your finite understanding.

Refuting Dependence of Attribute Duration
The author argues that attribute duration cannot depend on another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the duration of an attribute to anything other than the attribute itself leads to logical impossibility.

Core Idea
An attribute's duration must be intrinsic; depending on another leads to circularity or infinite regress.

This passage presents a concise rational argument from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. It examines the logical consequences of claiming that an attribute endures because of something else, showing that the only viable position is that the attribute's duration is inherent. Let's read the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: دوام, صفة, ذات, دور, تسلسل, محال.
The author uses a conditional structure 'if...then...'
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument presents two possible candidates for the 'something else' upon which the attribute's duration might depend: the essence (ذات) or something else. Trace each branch in the text.
The two branches are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
3
Understand the consequences
For each possibility, the author shows an impossible outcome: circularity if it's the essence, infinite regress if it's something else. Ask yourself why these are considered absolute impossibilities in kalam.
Kalam rules of reasoning treat دور and تسلسل as logical dead ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما إن كان دوام تلك الصفة بدوام شيء آخر ، فذلك الآخر إن كان هو الذات لزم الدور ، وإن كان شيئاً آخر لزم التسلسل ، والكل محال . فهذا حاصل الكلام المعقول الملخص في هذا الباب . والله أعلم
And if the duration of that attribute is due to the duration of something else, then if that something else is the essence, circularity follows; and if it is something else, infinite regress follows—and all are impossible. This is the gist of the refined rational discourse on this topic. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam argument against the idea that an attribute's eternity or duration depends on something other than the essence itself. The author reasons as follows: Consider an attribute that endures (i.e., is eternal) because of the duration of something else. There are only two possibilities for this 'something else': it is either the essence (ذات) itself or something distinct from the essence. If it is the essence, then the attribute's duration depends on the essence; but that means the attribute is not self-subsisting—this leads to circularity (دور) because the attribute would require the essence to persist, while the essence itself might be understood through the attribute. If it is something else, say another attribute or a separate entity, then that other thing must also have duration, which in turn depends on yet another thing, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). Both circularity and infinite regress are considered impossible (محال) in kalam, as they imply logical inconsistency. Therefore, the only coherent position is that the attribute's duration is intrinsic to the attribute itself. The author then closes by stating that this is the essence of the refined rational argument on this subject, and attributes ultimate knowledge to Allah.

Key Terms
دوام
صفة
ذات
دور
تسلسل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circularity and infinite regress are logical possibilities that could be avoided with careful reasoning.
Correction: In kalam, circularity and infinite regress are considered absolute impossibilities that render any argument invalid.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what are the two possibilities for the 'something else' upon which the duration of an attribute might depend?
A) The attribute itself or the essence
B) The essence or something other than the essence
C) Another attribute or the essence
D) Something contingent or something eternal

Key Takeaway
An attribute's duration cannot depend on anything other than itself without leading to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your religious life do you find yourself making the duration of your faith depend on something other than Allah—like your own actions, feelings, or the opinions of others? How does this lesson challenge you to realign your trust? Write down one specific change you will make this week to affirm that your faith's foundation is solely in Allah's intrinsic attributes.
?
The passage proves that any dependency besides the essence leads to logical impossibility. Is there a belief you hold where you unknowingly attribute independence to something other than Allah? Reflect on how you can root your certainty in Allah alone, and commit to one action that demonstrates this reliance.
?
If attribute duration depended on something else, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress—both impossible. How does this impact your understanding of tawheed in your daily life? Can you identify a practice or mindset that inadvertently implies such a dependency? What step will you take today to correct it?

The Meaning of God's Attribute of Life
The author defines God's life and responds to objections about its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's attribute of life as the capacity to know and have power, and how the author defends this definition against objections.

Core Idea
God’s life means He can know and will—not a separate attribute added to His essence.

This lesson examines the core meaning of the divine attribute of life (hayāt). The author begins by stating that being 'alive' means that it is valid for God to know and have power. Since His knowledge and power are already established, life is simply the affirmation of that capacity. However, objections arise: some argue that life must be a distinct existential attribute, not merely the capacity. The author addresses these objections, showing that the unique quiddity of God’s essence makes an additional attribute unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overall sense of the argument.
2
Identify the core definition
Notice the opening definition: life means the validity (ṣiḥḥa) of knowledge and power. This is the key claim.
3
Understand the objection
The passage presents an objection: impossibility is a negative concept, so its opposite would be non-existential, yet life is existential. Trace the logic.
4
See the refutation
The author responds by distinguishing the uniqueness of God's essence from other essences. Understand why the objection fails.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage upholds that life is not a separate attribute but the very capacity to know and will, grounded in the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المراد من كونه تعالى حياً : إنه يصح أن يعلم ويقدر، ولما ثبت بالدليل كونه قادراً عالماً. وكل ما كان موجوداً امتنع أن يكون ممتنع الوجود، يثبت أنه تعالى يصح أن يعلم ويقدر، ولا معنى لكونه حياً إلا ذلك. ولقائل أن يقول : الامتناع مفهوم عدمي، فنقي الامتناع يكون عدماً للعلم فيكون موجوداً وكونه حياً مفهوم وجودي، وأنه صفة للذات، ونعت لها، فيمتنع أن يكون عين الذات، فيكون هو صفة وجودية قائمة بالذات. ومن الناس من قال : الحياة صفة موجودة لأجلها يصح على الذات أن يعلم ويقدر. واحتج عليه : بأنه لولا اختصاص تلك الذات بالصفة التي لأجلها يصح عليها العلم والقدرة، لم يكن ثبوت هذه الصحة لتلك الذات أولى ثبوتها لسائر الذوات. ويقال له : هذا إنما يلزم لو قلنا : الذوات متساوية في الماهية، حتى يقال : لولا اختصاص بعضها بهذه الصفة ... وإلا لما حصل هذا الامتياز. أما لما كانت ذاته المخصوصة مخالفة بالماهية لسائر الذوات لم يلزم ما ذكرتموه.
Know that what is meant by His being, Exalted, 'alive' is that it is valid that He knows and has power. And since it has been established by proof that He is powerful and knowing, and everything that exists is impossible that it be impossible of existence, it is established that He, Exalted, is such that it is valid that He knows and has power, and there is no meaning for His being alive except that. And a speaker could say: Impossibility is a non-existential concept, so the opposite of impossibility is a non-existence of knowledge, so it would be existent? And His being alive is an existential concept, and it is an attribute of the essence, and a description of it, so it is impossible that it be identical to the essence, so it is an existential attribute subsisting in the essence. And among people are those who said: Life is an existing attribute on account of which it is valid for the essence to know and have power. And they argued for it: that if it were not for the specification of that essence by the attribute on account of which knowledge and power are valid, then the affirmation of this validity for that essence would not be more appropriate than its affirmation for other essences. And it is said to him: This only follows if we said that essences are equal in quiddity, so that it is said: if it were not for the specification of some of them by this attribute... otherwise this distinction would not occur. But when His specific essence is different in quiddity from other essences, what you mentioned does not follow.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life (hayāt) and addresses two related objections. The author begins by stating that God being 'alive' means that it is valid (yaṣiḥḥ) for Him to know (ya‘lam) and to have power (yaqdir). Since it has been proven that God is knowing and powerful, and since whatever exists cannot be non-existent, it follows that God is alive in this sense. The author then cites an objection: the concept of impossibility is negative (négatif), so its negation would be a lack of knowledge, which is non-existential, yet being alive is a positive (wujūdī) concept; therefore life cannot be identical to the essence but must be a positive attribute subsisting in the essence. The author then presents another view: some say life is a distinct attribute for the sake of which the essence can know and have power. Their proof is that without a specific attribute, the capacity to know and will would not be exclusive to God. The author refutes this by arguing that the proof only holds if all essences are equal in quiddity (māhiyya), but God's essence is unique and different in quiddity from all others, so the exclusive capacity follows from the essence itself, not from an additional attribute. Thus the author upholds the original definition of life as the capacity to know and will, without requiring a separate attribute.

حي
العلم
القدرة
صفة وجودية
الذات
الامتناع
مفهوم عدمي
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is a separate attribute added to God's essence.
Correction: Life is merely the capacity to know and will, grounded in the essence itself, not an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of God being 'alive'?
A) An additional attribute subsisting in the essence
B) The capacity to know and have power
C) The negation of impossibility
D) A non-existential concept

Key Takeaway
God’s life means He can know and have power—this is not a separate attribute but the nature of His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author argues that God's life is nothing other than the validity of His knowledge and power. How can you unify your worship and reliance on God as a single, undivided reality?

The Meaning of God's Life
Sanusi defines God's life and presents an objection that it might be a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's attribute of life as the validity to know and will, and the challenge that life might be a distinct positive attribute.

Core Idea
God's life means He can know and will; it is not a separate attribute but the essence itself.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah introduces the concept of God's life (hayat). The author begins by defining what it means for God to be alive: it is that it is valid for Him to know and to will. However, a potential objection is raised: if life is simply the negation of impossibility, it would be negative, but life is understood as a positive attribute. This lesson explores the initial definition and the challenge that life might be a distinct entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
The passage defines life as the validity to know and will. Note the use of 'يصح أن يعلم ويقدر'.
The definition is concise: life is nothing more than this capability.
2
Notice the objection
An objection is raised: impossibility is negative, so its negation is also negative, but life is positive; thus life must be a separate attribute.
This objection challenges the initial definition.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author presents this objection without immediate answer; it sets up the discussion for the next lesson.
The objection is a common theological concern.

اعلم أن المراد من كونه تعالى حياً : إنه يصح أن يعلم ويقدر ، ولما ثبت بالدليل كونه قادراً عالماً . وكل ما كان موجوداً امتنع أن يكون ممتنع الوجود ، يثبت أنه تعالى يصح أن يعلم ويقدر ، ولا معنى لكونه حياً إلا ذلك . ولقائل أن يقول : الامتناع مفهوم عدمي ، فنقي الامتناع يكون عدماً للعلم فيكون موجوداً وكونه حياً مفهوم وجودي ، وأنه صفة للذات ، ونعت لها ، فيمتنع أن يكون عين الذات ، فيكون هو صفة وجودية قائمة بالذات . ومن الناس من قال : الحياة صفة موجودة لأجلها يصح على الذات أن يعلم ويقدر . واحتج عليه : بأنه لولا اختصاص تلك الذات بالصفة التي لأجلها يصح عليها العلم والقدرة ، لم يكن ثبوت هذه الصحة لتلك الذات أولى ثبوتها لسائر الذوات . ويقال له : هذا إنما يلزم لو قلنا : الذوات متساوية في الماهية ، حتى يقال : لولا اختصاص بعضها بهذه الصفة ... وإلا لما حصل هذا الامتياز . أما لما كانت ذاته المخصوصة مخالفة بالماهية لسائر الذوات لم يلزم ما ذكرتموه (۱) عنونا بالباب السابع بدل الفصل الثاني عشر . (۲) زيادة (۳) الصفة ( س ) . وأيضاً : كما أن الذوات مختلفة في صحة أن يعلم ويقدر ، فكذلك مختلفة في صحة أن يكون حياً فإن وجب أن يعلل ذلك الاختلاف بصفة ، لوجب أن يعلل اختلافها في صحة الحياة بصفة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن قلتم : بأن ذلك الاختلاف معلل بالذات المخصوصة . فلم لا يجوز مثله في صحة العالمية والقادرية ؟ والله أعلم .
Know that what is meant by His being, may He be exalted, 'alive' is that it is valid for Him to know and to will. And since it is established by proof that He is powerful and knowing, and everything that exists cannot be impossible of existence, it is established that He, may He be exalted, is valid for Him to know and to will, and there is no meaning to His being alive except that. Someone may say: Impossibility is a negative concept, so the negation of impossibility would be a negation of knowledge, thus it would be existent, while His being alive is a positive concept, and it is an attribute of the essence and a description of it, so it is impossible that it be the essence itself; rather it is a positive attribute subsisting through the essence. And among people are those who said: Life is an existent attribute for which it is valid for the essence to know and to will. And he argued: If there were no specification of that essence with the attribute for which knowledge and power are valid for it, then the establishment of this validity for that essence would not be more deserving than its establishment for other essences. It is said to him: This only follows if we say that essences are equal in quiddity, so that it could be said: without the specification of some of them with this attribute... otherwise this distinction would not occur. But since His particular essence is different in quiddity from other essences, what you have mentioned does not follow. (1) We titled it as Chapter Seven instead of Chapter Twelve. (2) Addition. (3) The attribute (S). And also: Just as essences differ in the validity to know and to will, so too they differ in the validity to be alive. So if it is necessary to explain that difference by an attribute, then it would be necessary to explain their difference in the validity of life by another attribute, leading to infinite regress. And if you say that that difference is explained by the particular essence, then why is the like not permissible in the validity of being knowing and powerful? And God knows best.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of life (hayat) for God. It begins by defining what it means for God to be 'alive': it means that it is valid for Him to know (yā'lam) and to will (yaqdir). Since it has been proven by evidence that God is powerful and knowing, and since whatever exists cannot be impossible of existence, it is established that it is valid for Him to know and will. The author asserts that this is the only meaning of life—the capability to know and will. Then an objection is raised: impossibility is a negative concept; its negation would also be negative, so if life is merely the negation of impossibility, it would be a negative concept. But life is understood as a positive concept and an attribute of the essence, so it cannot be the essence itself; rather it must be a positive attribute subsisting through the essence. The objection implies that life is a distinct attribute. Then a second opinion is presented: some say life is an existent attribute for which the essence becomes capable of knowing and willing. The argument for this opinion is that without a specific attribute qualifying the essence, the capability to know and will would be equally applicable to all essences, leading to no distinction. The author rebuts this by saying that this argument only holds if all essences were equal in quiddity (māhiyya). But God's essence is unique and different in quiddity from all other essences, so the capability to know and will can be intrinsic to His essence without needing a separate attribute of life. Furthermore, the author argues by analogy: if difference in capability to know and will requires a distinct attribute, then difference in capability to be alive would also require another attribute, leading to infinite regress. If one says the difference is due to the particular essence itself, then why not allow the same for knowledge and power? The passage concludes with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
حياة
قادر
عالِم
صفة وجودية
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's life must be a separate attribute because it is a positive concept.
Correction: The passage shows that life is defined as the capability to know and will, which is a positive state, but it may be identical to the essence's capability.

Checkpoint
According to the initial definition, what does it mean for God to be alive?
A) He has a distinct attribute of life
B) It is valid for Him to know and to will
C) He negates impossibility
D) He is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's life is defined as the validity to know and will, but an objection argues that it must be a distinct positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life is nothing but the fact that He can know and will for you, why do you still act as if your own plans and knowledge are what ultimately determine your success? When was the last time you genuinely surrendered a decision to God's will, trusting that His life means He is actively knowing and willing what is best for you—even when it contradicts your own reasoning?
?
The objector in this lesson insists that a positive attribute must be distinct from the essence. But what if the very attribute you rely on—God's life—is not a separate thing but His very being? Would you live differently if you believed that God's knowledge and will are not just something He does but who He is? What would change in your prayer, your trust, or your fear of Him?
?
You have just read that life means the validity to know and will. Does your daily life reflect that you believe God is alive in this sense—that He is constantly knowing and willing every detail of your existence? Or do you functionally live as if He is distant or uninvolved? Be honest: where is the disconnect between your confession and your conduct?

Life as a Distinct Attribute: Argument and Rebuttal
Sanusi presents and refutes the view that life is a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that life is a distinct attribute, its argument based on necessity of specification, and the author's rebuttal using essence uniqueness and infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's life is not a separate attribute; His unique essence itself grounds knowledge and power.

This lesson continues the discussion on God's life. Some theologians argue that life must be a distinct attribute to specify which essences can know and will. The author rebuts this by asserting that God's essence is unique and different in quiddity, and that requiring a distinct attribute would lead to infinite regress. This lesson explores this argument and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the second opinion
Note that some say life is an existent attribute that enables knowledge and will.
This is the distinct attribute view.
2
Understand the argument
The argument claims that without a specific attribute, the validity to know and will would be equally applicable to all essences, so a distinction is needed.
The argument relies on the need for specification.
3
Follow the rebuttal
The author responds by saying that essences are not equal; God's essence is unique. Also, if difference in validity requires an attribute, then difference in life itself would require another attribute, leading to infinite regress.
The rebuttal uses the uniqueness of God's essence and a regress argument.
4
Reflect on the analogy
If one says the essence itself explains the difference, then why not for knowledge and power?
This closes the argument.

Argument on Attributes and Essence
An argument against attributing differences in life, knowledge, and power to separate attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a kalam argument that refutes the attribution of differences in essential capacities to separate attributes, using the problem of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Differences in essential capacities cannot be explained by separate attributes without infinite regress or inconsistency.

This passage presents a concise but powerful argument from a classical kalam text against a specific view on divine attributes. The argument challenges the idea that the differences in essences' capacities (like being knowing, powerful, alive) require separate explanatory attributes. By examining two possible explanations, the author shows that each leads to an unacceptable consequence, thereby defending the position that these differences are rooted in the essence itself. Understanding this argument is key to appreciating the coherent simplicity of the divine essence central to Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end: 'Why is the like of it not permissible?'
2
Identify the two options
The passage presents two alternative explanations for the difference in essential capacities: (1) by an attribute, (2) by the specific essence. Identify where each is mentioned.
The first option is introduced with 'فإن وجب أن يعلل ذلك الاختلاف بصفة', the second with 'وإن قلتم بأن ذلك الاختلاف معلل بالذات المخصوصة'.
3
Analyze the first option and its consequence
If the difference is caused by an attribute, then that attribute itself would need explanation, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Understand why this is unacceptable in kalam.
Infinite regress is considered impossible in proving the existence of God or attributes.
4
Analyze the second option and its implication
If the difference is due to the specific essence, then why not apply the same reasoning to the capacities for knowledge and power? This would undermine the need for distinct attributes.
The author uses consistency to argue that the opponent's position is arbitrary.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument implies that the original view (attributing differences to separate attributes) is untenable. The essence itself suffices to explain the differences in capacities.
The passage ends with 'And God knows best', indicating the author's confidence in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : كما أن الذوات مختلفة في صحة أن يعلم ويقدر ، فكذلك مختلفة في صحة أن يكون حياً فإن وجب ) أن يعلل ذلك الاختلاف بصفة ، لوجب أن يعلل اختلافها في صحة الحياة ) بصفة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن قلتم : بأن ذلك الاختلاف معلل بالذات المخصوصة . فلم لا يجوز مثله في صحة العالمية والقادرية ؟ والله أعلم .
And also: just as essences (al-dhāt) differ in the possibility of being knowing and powerful, so they differ in the possibility of being alive. So if it is necessary that this difference be caused (mu‘allal) by an attribute, then it would be necessary that their difference in the possibility of life be caused by another attribute, and [this would] entail infinite regress. And if you say: that difference is caused by the specific essence (al-dhāt al-makhṣūṣa), then why is the like of it not permissible in the possibility of being knowing and powerful? And God knows best.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument that refutes a particular position on divine attributes. The author begins by stating a premise: just as essences differ in their capacity to have knowledge and power, they also differ in their capacity to have life. Then the argument proceeds by considering two options: if these differences are explained by additional attributes, then the difference in the capacity for life would require yet another attribute, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is absurd. Alternatively, if one argues that the difference is due to the specific essence itself, then consistency demands that the same reasoning apply to the capacities for knowledge and power—i.e., those differences would also be due to the essence alone, undermining the need for distinct attributes. The conclusion, implied by the author, is that the original position is untenable. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
الذوات (al-dhāt)
صحة (ṣiḥḥa, possibility/capacity)
عالمية (ʿālamiyya, being knowing)
قادرية (qādariyya, being powerful)
حياة (ḥayāh, life)
تعليل (taʿlīl, causation/explanation)
تسلسل (tasalsul, infinite regress)
ذات مخصوصة (dhāt makhṣūṣa, specific essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument rejects the existence of all divine attributes (like knowledge, power, life).
Correction: The argument only rejects that differences in essential capacities are caused by separate attributes. It does not deny the attributes themselves; rather, it asserts that the essence itself possesses these capacities inherently.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author use to refute the claim that differences in essential capacities are caused by attributes?
A) It leads to the denial of divine simplicity.
B) It results in an infinite regress of attributes.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It implies that God is composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
The argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress and the requirement of consistency to show that differences in essential capacities cannot be explained by additional attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever accept inconsistent explanations because they are familiar? This argument forces consistency. Where in your own understanding of Allah might you be tolerating contradictions?
?
If the infinite regress is absurd in theology, why do you sometimes create endless justifications for your own shortcomings instead of attributing them to your own essence?
?
The argument shows that differences in capacities cannot be outsourced to separate attributes. In your life, do you attribute your failures and successes to external factors rather than taking ownership of your own character?

Divine Attributes
