The Eight Attributes and the Logic of Limitation
Enumeration of eight attributes and refutation of adding more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī position on the eight divine attributes and the rational argument for not affirming additional attributes beyond what proof establishes.

Core Idea
Proof alone determines which attributes we affirm; beyond proof, we must suspend judgment.

This passage from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah presents the standard Ashʿarī enumeration of eight divine attributes—life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, seeing, speech, and everlastingness—and defends the limitation against the objection that more could be affirmed. The author does not simply assert the number; he provides a rational argument that permitting unproven attributes leads to absurd infinite possibilities. He then refines the argument using logical principles from the science of logic, teaching a lesson about epistemic humility: lack of proof does not prove non-existence, but it does require suspension of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the author's style: he states the position, presents an objection-and-answer pair, then adds a logical refinement.
The Arabic contains technical terms; don't worry about every word yet.
2
Identify the eight attributes
Find the list of eight attributes and note how they are defined. This is the standard Ashʿarī catalogue.
They are: living, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing, speaking, everlasting.
3
Understand the primary argument
The mutakallimun say: proof indicates only these eight; what is not proven must be denied. This is their first line of reasoning.
They do not claim that lack of proof alone proves non-existence—they have a secondary argument about infinite absurdity.
4
Follow the secondary argument and its critique
The secondary argument says: if we affirm without proof, any number of attributes would be equally possible, leading to infinite unknown attributes. The author then critiques this argument using logical distinctions, showing it only leads to suspension of judgment, not to a positive conclusion.
The author distinguishes between 'priority in the mind' and 'priority in reality'.
5
Grasp the final methodological rule
The author concludes: affirm what reason proves, deny what reason disproves, and suspend judgment where reason is silent. This is the correct epistemic stance.
This rule applies beyond divine attributes to all theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المتكلمين حصروا الصفات في هذه الثمانية. وهي كونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، مريداً، سميعاً بصيراً، متكلماً، باقياً. فإذا قيل لهم: فهل تثبتون الله صفة أخرى؟ قالوا: لا. لأن الدليل لم يدل إلا على هذه الصفات، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه. وربما قالوا: لو جوزنا إثبات ما لا دليل عليه لم يكن عدد أولى من عدد آخر، فيلزم إثبات أعداد لا نهاية لها من الصفات المجهولة. وذلك محال. وقد علمت في علم المنطق: أن عدم العلم بالشيء لا يفيد العلم بعدمه. وعلمت أيضاً: أن قول القائل: ليس عدد أولى من عدد. إن أريد به هذه الأولوية في الذهن والعقل، فذاك لا يفيد إلا التوقف وعدم الجزم. وإن أريد به عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر، فهذا مما لم يمكن إثباته، بل الواجب أن يقال: إن ما دل العقل على ثبوته قضينا بثبوته وما لم يدل العقل على ثبوته ولا على عدمه وجب التوقف فيه. والله أعلم.
Know that the mutakallimun (theologians) restricted the attributes (al-ṣifāt) to these eight: that He is living (ḥayy), knowing (ʿālim), powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), hearing (samīʿ), seeing (baṣīr), speaking (mutakallim), and everlasting (bāqī). If it is said to them: 'Do you affirm another attribute of Allah?' They say: 'No, because the proof (dalīl) has not indicated except these attributes, and what is not proven must be denied (nafy).' And perhaps they said: 'If we permitted affirming what is not proven, then no number would be more appropriate than another number, and this would entail affirming an infinite number of unknown attributes. That is absurd (muḥāl).' You have learned in logic (ʿilm al-manṭiq) that ignorance of a thing does not yield knowledge of its non-existence. And you have also learned that the statement of the speaker: 'No number is more appropriate than another number'—if what is intended by it is this priority in the mind and intellect, then that yields only suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) and lack of certainty. And if what is intended by it is the absence of priority in reality (fī nafs al-amr), then this is something that cannot be proven. Rather, it must be said: 'What the intellect indicates to be proven, we judge its existence; and what the intellect does not indicate to be proven nor non-existent, suspension of judgment is necessary.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a proof combined with an objection and its answer. It begins by stating that the mutakallimun (theologians) have limited the divine attributes to eight: life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, seeing, speech, and eternal duration. This is a standard enumeration in Ashʿarī theology. The text then presents an objection that could be raised: why not affirm additional attributes? The theologians respond that only these eight are proven by evidence (dalīl), and whatever is not proven must be denied. They offer a further argument: if we allowed affirming what is not proven, then no particular number of attributes would be more appropriate than any other; this would lead to an infinite number of unknown attributes, which is absurd. The author then refines this argument by drawing on logical principles. First, he notes that lack of knowledge of something does not imply knowledge of its absence. Second, he analyzes the claim 'no number is more appropriate than another.' If this means that the mind cannot decide between numbers, that only leads to suspension of judgment (tawaqquf), not to a positive conclusion. If it means that in objective reality there is no priority, that itself cannot be proven. The correct approach, the author concludes, is to affirm what reason proves, deny what reason disproves, and suspend judgment on matters that reason neither proves nor disproves. Thus, the eight attributes are affirmed because proof exists for them; additional attributes are not affirmed for lack of proof, and the argument from inappropriate numbers is insufficient to necessitate negation. The author ends with 'And Allah knows best.'

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the mutakallimun deny extra attributes simply because there is no proof, which would mean 'lack of evidence is evidence of absence'.
Correction: The passage explicitly teaches that ignorance of a thing does not yield knowledge of its non-existence. The mutakallimun do not claim that lack of proof alone proves non-existence; rather, they use a secondary argument about infinite absurdity and ultimately require suspension of judgment when proof is absent.

Checkpoint

B) Because proof (dalīl) has only indicated these eight attributes.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of more than eight attributes.
D) Because any addition would lead to an infinite regress of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī position on divine attributes is not arbitrary; it is grounded in proof (dalīl), and where proof is absent, the correct epistemic stance is suspension of judgment (tawaqquf).

The Eight Attributes and the Principle of Evidence
The author enumerates the eight attributes and presents the theologians' argument against adding more without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that divine attributes are limited to eight based on evidence, and why affirming without evidence leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Only evidence can establish a divine attribute; otherwise, infinite unknown attributes would follow.

The author begins by stating the classical Sunni position that God’s attributes are confined to eight. He then presents the theologians’ argument: since only these eight are indicated by evidence, any additional attribute must be denied. They further argue that affirming without evidence would lead to an infinite regress of unknown attributes, which is impossible. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the eight attributes
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'وذلك محال'. Notice the enumeration and the argument.
The enumeration is a list: حي, عالم, قادر, مريد, سميع, بصير, متكلم, باق.
2
Understand the evidence principle
The theologians say 'because the evidence indicates only these attributes, and what there is no evidence for must be negated.' This is a key methodological rule.
This principle is derived from the fact that we only know God through revelation and reason.
3
Grasp the reductio argument
If we allowed affirming without evidence, then no number would be more fitting than another, leading to infinite unknown attributes. This shows the absurdity of affirming without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأولي
في جهر صفات الله تعالى
اعلم أن المتكلمين حصروا الصفات في هذه الثمانية . وهي كونه حياً ، عالماً ، قادراً ، مريداً ، سميعاً بصيراً ، متكلماً ، باقياً . فإذا قيل لهم : فهل تثبتون الله صفة أخرى ؟ قالوا : لا . لأن الدليل لم يدل إلا على هذه الصفات، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه . وربما قالوا : لو جوزنا إثبات ما لا دليل عليه لم يكن عدد أولى من عدد آخر ، فيلزم إثبات أعداد لا نهاية لها من الصفات المجهولة . وذلك محال
الشيء
عدم
وقد علمت في علم المنطق : أن عدم العلم بالشيء لا يفيد العلم بعلم
وعلمت أيضاً : أن قول القائل : ليس عدد أولى من عدد . أن أريد به هذه الأولوية في الذهن والعقل ، فذاك لا يفيد إلا التوقف وعدم الجزم . وإن أريد به عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ، فهذا مما لم يمكن إثباته ، بل الواجب أن يقال : إن ما دل العقل على ثبوته قضينا بثبوته وما لم يدل العقل على ثبوته ولا على عدمه وجب التوقف فيه . والله أعلم .
Chapter One
On the mention of the attributes of God, the Exalted.
Know that the theologians have confined the attributes to these eight: that He is living, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing, speaking, and eternal. If it is said to them: 'Do you affirm for God any other attribute?' They say: 'No, because the evidence indicates only these attributes, and what there is no evidence for must be negated.' And perhaps they say: 'If we permitted affirming what there is no evidence for, then no number would be more fitting than another, leading to the affirmation of infinite numbers of unknown attributes, and that is impossible.'
[?]
[?]
And you have learned in the science of logic: that lack of knowledge of a thing does not yield knowledge of non-existence.
And you also learned: that one's saying 'no number is more fitting than another' – if by that is meant this priority in the mind and intellect, then that only yields suspension of judgment and lack of certainty. But if by that is meant lack of priority in actual reality, then this is something that cannot be proven. Rather, the obligatory position is to say: What the intellect indicates as existing, we judge to exist, and what the intellect does not indicate as existing nor as non-existing, suspension of judgment is necessary concerning it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is both a classification and an argument. It begins by stating that the theologians have confined the attributes of God to eight specific ones: living, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing, speaking, and eternal. Then it addresses an objection: why not affirm additional attributes? The theologians respond by invoking the principle of evidence: only what the evidence proves is affirmed; what lacks evidence must be denied. They also present a reductio ad absurdum: if one allowed affirming without evidence, then there would be no basis to prefer one number of attributes over another, leading to an infinite regress of unknown attributes, which is impossible. The author then interjects a logical point: ignorance of something does not constitute knowledge of its non-existence. He further analyzes the argument 'no number is more fitting than another': if this means no number is preferable in the mind, then it only leads to suspension of judgment; if it means no number is actually preferable in reality, then that claim itself cannot be proven. Therefore, the correct approach is to affirm only what reason proves and to suspend judgment on what reason neither proves nor disproves. This passage thus reinforces the Sunni methodological principle that affirmation of divine attributes is limited to what is established by evidence.

Key Takeaway
Affirming a divine attribute requires evidence; otherwise, it leads to an infinite regress of unknown attributes.

The Proper Method: Logical Correction
The author clarifies the logical principle and the correct method for assessing claims about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that lack of knowledge does not imply non-existence, and the correct method of suspending judgment on matters not proven or disproven by reason.

Core Idea
Ignorance of something is not proof of its non-existence.

After presenting the theologians’ argument, the author adds an important logical clarification from the science of logic: not knowing something does not mean it does not exist. He then critiques the phrase 'no number is more fitting than another' and explains the proper epistemological stance: affirm what reason proves, deny what reason disproves, and suspend judgment on the rest. This lesson unpacks that refined methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical principle
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وقد علمت' to the end. Note the first principle: lack of knowledge does not give knowledge of non-existence.
This is a standard logical maxim.
2
Analyze the phrase 'no number is more fitting'
The author distinguishes two interpretations: in the mind vs. in reality. The first leads only to suspension; the second cannot be proven.
This shows careful logical analysis.
3
Learn the proper method
The author concludes: affirm what reason proves, deny what reason disproves, and suspend judgment otherwise. This is the correct approach to divine attributes.

The Necessity of Relations for Divine Knowledge and Power
Author defines qadir and 'alim, argues for necessary relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why logical relations (nisab/idafat) must be affirmed for God to be described as knowing and powerful, and how the author argues this point.

Core Idea
Relations are the necessary bridge between God's essence and His descriptions as Knower and Powerful.

This passage from al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah tackles a fundamental question in Islamic theology: Are God's knowledge and power identical to His essence or distinct? The author begins by defining key terms and argues that specific relations between God and His creation must be affirmed; otherwise, we cannot truly say God is knowing and powerful. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and argument come through.
Pay attention to the definitions of 'qadir' and 'alim'.
2
Identify the definitions
Find the author's definitions of 'powerful' (al-qadir) and 'knower' (al-'alim). Notice that both involve a relation: validity (sihha) for power, and awareness (shu'ur) and discrimination (tabayyun) for knowledge.
3
Understand the necessity of relations
The author asserts that awareness and discrimination only occur through a specific relation between the knower and the known. Ask yourself: why does the author think these relations (nisab, idafat) must be affirmed?
The consequence of denying them is the impossibility of acknowledging God as powerful and knowing.
4
Examine the three interpretations
The author lists three possible meanings for 'God is knowing and powerful' and shows that only the first two are acceptable; the third requires separate investigation. Notice how the author ties each interpretation to the affirmation or negation of relations.
The first interpretation ('God has knowledge and power') is undeniable. The second ('knower' and 'powerful' as essence-with-relations) makes relations necessary. The third (some other meaning) is left aside.
5
Consider the mutakallimun view
The passage introduces a view from some mutakallimun who posit three things: the essence, the attribute (knowledge), and its attachment to the known. Understand how this view relates to the earlier argument.
The text ends abruptly, but this view is presented as an example of affirming relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في أنه تعالى عالم لذاته أو لعنى؟ التنان أو اعلم (1) . أن أهم المهمات في هذه المسائل البحث عن محل الخلاف فنقول : لا شك أن القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل . وهذه الصحة ليست نفس تلك الذات المخصوصة، لأن المفهوم من هذه الصحة قد يعلمه من لا يعلم حقيقة تلك الذات المخصوصة . وأيضاً : العالم هو الذي يكون له شعور بذلك الشيء ، وتبين به. وقد عرفت أن معنى الشعور والإدراك والنبيين لا يحصل البتة إلا عند حصول نسبة مخصوصة بين ذات العالم وبين المعلوم . وهذه النسب والإضافات المسماة بالتعلق لا بد من إثباتها ، وإلا فيمتنع الاعتراف يكونه تعالي قادراً عالماً ، فإن كان المراد بقولنا : إنه تعالى عالم وله علم . وقادر وله قدرة هذا المعنى فذلك مما لا سبيل البتة إلى إنكاره ، وإن كان معنى العالم هو الذات الموصوفة بهذه النسبة الخاصة ، ومعنى القادر هو الذات الموصوفة بتلك الصحة المخصوصة ، كان نفي هذه النسب ، وهذه الإضافات نفياً لكونه عالماً قادراً . وإن كان المراد منه معنى آخر وراء ما ذكرناه فذلك يستدعي بحثاً آخر ، سوى ما ذكرناه . وتقريره : إن من المتكلمين من زعم : أن العلم صفة قائمة بذات العالم ، ولها تعلق بالمعلوم. وهذا القائل أثبت أموراً ثلاثة : أحدها الذات . وثانيها : (1) الرابع عشر [ الأصل ] .
The Second Chapter: On whether He, exalted, is knowing by His essence or by a meaning? The two [views] or know (1). That the most important of the important matters in these issues is the investigation of the point of disagreement. So we say: There is no doubt that the powerful (al-qadir) is the one from whom the act is valid (yasihhu). And this validity (sihha) is not that specific essence itself, because the concept of this validity may be known by one who does not know the reality of that specific essence. Also: The knower (al-'alim) is the one who has awareness (shu'ur) of that thing and discrimination (tabayyun) by it. And you have known that the meaning of awareness, perception, and discrimination does not occur at all except when a specific relation (nisba makhsusa) occurs between the essence of the knower and the known. And these relations (nisab) and additions (idafat), called attachment (ta'allaq), must be affirmed; otherwise, acknowledgment of His being exalted powerful and knowing is impossible. For if what is meant by our saying: 'He, exalted, is knowing and has knowledge, and powerful and has power' is this meaning, then that is in no way possible to deny. And if the meaning of 'knower' is the essence described by this specific relation, and the meaning of 'powerful' is the essence described by that specific validity, then the negation of these relations and these additions is a negation of His being knowing and powerful. And if what is meant is another meaning beyond what we have mentioned, then that requires another investigation besides what we have mentioned. And its exposition: Indeed, among the mutakallimun there is one who claimed that knowledge is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the essence of the knower, and it has attachment to the known. And this claimant affirmed three things: one of them is the essence; and the second is: (1) The fourteenth [in the original].
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the nature of divine knowledge and power, particularly whether these are identical to God's essence or distinct attributes. The author begins by stating the importance of identifying the precise point of disagreement (mahall al-khilaf). He then defines key terms: 'powerful' (al-qadir) is that from which action is valid (yasihhu al-fi'l). This 'validity' (sihha) is not the same as the specific essence, because one can understand the concept of validity without knowing the essence itself. Similarly, 'knower' (al-'alim) is that which has awareness and discrimination of a thing. The author then asserts that awareness, perception, and discrimination only occur when there is a specific relation (nisba makhsusa) between the knower and the known. These relations or additions (nisab/idafat), called 'attachment' (ta'allaq), must be affirmed; otherwise, it would be impossible to acknowledge that God is powerful and knowing. The author then clarifies two possible interpretations: (1) If by saying 'God is knowing and has knowledge' we mean that He has a relation or attribute, that is undeniable. (2) If 'knower' means the essence described by the relation, and 'powerful' means the essence described by validity, then denying these relations would be denying that God is knowing and powerful. If someone means something else, that requires separate discussion. The author then introduces a view from the mutakallimun: some claim that knowledge is a quality (sifa) subsisting in the essence of the knower, with attachment to the known. This claimant affirms three things: the essence, the attribute (presumably knowledge), and the attachment. The text ends abruptly with a footnote marker.

شعور

تبيين

A) The one who has the ability to act.
B) The one from whom the act is valid (yasihhu).
C) The one whose essence is power.
D) The one who creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
Affirming specific relations between God and His creation is logically necessary for describing Him as knowing and powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you pray differently tonight knowing this?

The Essential Meaning of God's Power and Knowledge
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi clarifies the essential meaning of God being knowing and powerful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attributes of power and knowledge necessarily involve relations (nisab) between God and His actions/objects, and why these relations must be affirmed.

Core Idea
Knowing and powerful are not just essences; they require relations to what is known and done.

Before we can debate whether God has eternal attributes, we must first agree on what it means for Him to be 'knowing' and 'powerful.' Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by defining these terms in a way that exposes the conceptual commitments of any claim about divine attributes. This passage sets the terms of debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very logical structure: definition, argument, enumeration.
2
Identify the definitions of qadir and alim
Find the definitions given for 'qadir' (powerful) and 'alim' (knowing). Note what each definition includes and excludes.
qadir is defined by possibility of action; alim by awareness/discernment.
3
Trace the argument for necessary relations
Notice how the author argues that possibility (sihhah) is not identical to the essence, and that awareness requires a specific relation between knower and known.
This establishes that the concepts involve relational aspects.
4
Understand the three possibilities presented
The author presents three options for what is meant by 'God is knowing/has knowledge'. Understand each option and why the first two are acceptable while the third requires further debate.
This frames the dispute: either accept relations or propose an alternative.
5
Reflect on the foundational nature of this argument
Ask yourself why this conceptual clarification is necessary before discussing divine attributes. How does it shift the burden of proof to those who deny relations?
Connect to the broader theological debates on divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن أهم المهمات في هذه المسائل البحث عن محل الخلاف فنقول : لا شك أن القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل . وهذه الصحة ليست نفس تلك الذات المخصوصة، لأن المفهوم من هذه الصحة قد يعلمه من لا يعلم حقيقة تلك الذات المخصوصة . وأيضاً : العالم هو الذي يكون له شعور بذلك الشيء ، وتبين به. وقد عرفت أن معنى الشعور والإدراك والنبيين لا يحصل البتة إلا عند حصول نسبة مخصوصة بين ذات العالم وبين المعلوم . وهذه النسب والإضافات المسماة بالتعلق لا بد من إثباتها ، وإلا فيمتنع الاعتراف يكونه تعالي قادراً عالماً ، فإن كان المراد بقولنا : إنه تعالى عالم وله علم . وقادر وله قدرة هذا المعنى فذلك مما لا سبيل البتة إلى إنكاره ، وإن كان معنى العالم هو الذات الموصوفة بهذه النسبة الخاصة ، ومعنى القادر هو الذات الموصوفة بتلك الصحة المخصوصة ، كان نفي هذه النسب ، وهذه الإضافات نفياً لكونه عالماً قادراً . وإن كان المراد منه معنى آخر وراء ما ذكرناه فذلك يستدعي بحثاً آخر ، سوى ما ذكرناه .
Know that the most important of the important matters in these issues is the investigation of the locus of dispute. We say: There is no doubt that the powerful (al-qadir) is the one from whom action is possible. This possibility (al-sihhah) is not the same as that particular essence, because the concept of this possibility may be known by one who does not know the reality of that particular essence. Also: the knowing (al-alim) is the one who has awareness (shu'ur) of that thing and discerns (tabayyun) it. And you have known that the meaning of awareness, perception, and discernment does not occur at all except when a specific relation (nisbah makhsusah) occurs between the essence of the knower and the known. These relations and additions (al-idafat), called 'attachment' (al-ta'alluq), must be affirmed, for otherwise acknowledging Him to be powerful and knowing is impossible. For if what is meant by our saying 'He, exalted, is knowing and has knowledge, and powerful and has power' is this meaning, then there is absolutely no way to deny it. And if the meaning of 'knowing' is the essence described by this specific relation, and the meaning of 'powerful' is the essence described by that specific possibility, then denial of these relations and additions is a denial of His being knowing and powerful. And if what is meant is another meaning beyond what we have mentioned, then that requires another investigation beyond what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual clarification and argument for the necessity of affirming relations (nisab/idafat) in the attributes of God. The author begins by stating that the most important task in these issues is to identify the exact point of disagreement. He then defines 'al-qadir' (the powerful) as the one from whom action is possible (yasihh minhu al-fi'l). He argues that this 'sihhah' (possibility) is not identical to the essence of the agent, because its concept can be understood without understanding the essence. Similarly, he defines 'al-alim' (the knowing) as the one who has awareness (shu'ur) and discernment (tabayyun) of something. The author states that awareness, perception, and discernment only occur when there is a specific relation (nisba makhsūṣa) between the knower's essence and the known. These relations and additions, called 'ta'alluq' (attachment), must be affirmed; otherwise, acknowledging God as powerful and knowing would be impossible. He then presents three possibilities for what is meant by the statements 'God is knowing and has knowledge' and 'God is powerful and has power': First, if the intended meaning is exactly this relation-based understanding, then it cannot be denied. Second, if 'knowing' means the essence described by the relation, and 'powerful' means the essence described by the possibility, then denying the relations is equivalent to denying the attributes. Third, if the opponent means something entirely different, then that requires a separate discussion. The author thus sets up a dilemma: either the opponent accepts the necessity of these relations or they must offer an alternative coherent meaning, shifting the burden of proof.

Correction: The author clarifies that the concept of possibility/awareness is known independently of the essence, and that awareness requires a specific relation between the knower and the known. Hence, the attributes inherently involve relations.

Positions on the Number of Entities in Divine Attributes
Al-Razi enumerates the different positions on the number of entities in divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on the number of entities in divine attributes and the author's own stance affirming only essence and relation.

Core Idea
Theologians disagree on how many distinct entities are involved in a divine attribute; al-Razi reduces them to two.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a range of views among theologians regarding how many separate things (amur) must be affirmed when discussing divine attributes like knowledge. He shows how each view logically expands the number of entities, and then states his own minimalist position. This sets the stage for his investigation into purely conceptual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Get a sense of the flow of positions.
Notice that the author uses a very structured enumeration of views.
2
Identify the first position (three entities)
Find the theologians who affirm three things: essence, attribute, and attachment. Note how each is defined.
3
Identify the second position (four or five entities)
These theologians add 'worldliness' (al-alamiyya) and potentially two attachments. Note the uncertainty al-Razi expresses about their exact doctrine.
Al-Razi presents two possible sub-views within this group.
4
Identify the author's own position (two entities)
Al-Razi affirms only essence and relation (nisba), which he calls worldliness. He insists the relation is additional to the essence.
This is his minimal ontological commitment.
5
Reflect on the term 'pure conceptual'
Al-Razi describes this investigation as 'al-ma'qul al-sirf'. Consider how this frames the discussion as philosophical theology, not verbal dispute.
This distinction will be important later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقريره : إن من المتكلمين من زعم : أن العلم صفة قائمة بذات العلم، ولها تعلق بالمعلوم. وهذا القائل أثبت أموراً ثلاثة : أحدها الذات . وثانيها : الصفة . وثالثها : التعلق الحاصل بين تلك الصفة وبين ذلك المعلوم . ومنهم من زعم أن العلم صفة توجب العالمية، ثم إنهم أثبتوا تعلقاً العالم وبين المعلوم، ولا أعرف كيفية مذاهبهم فيه . فإنه يحتمل أن يقولوا : المتعلقة بالمعلوم لا العلم، ويحتمل أن يقولوا : العلم هو المتعلق بالمعلوم، لا العالمية . وعلى هذه التقديرات فقد أثبتوا أموراً أربعة : الذات، والعلم، والعالمية، والتعلق . ولا يبعد أن يثبتوا التعلق للعلم وللعالمية أيضاً . وعلى هذا التقدير فقد أثبتوا أموراً خمسة . وأما نحن فلا نثبت إلا أمرين الذات والنسبة، المسماة بالعالمية، وندعي أن هذه النسبة ليست نفس الذات، بل هي أمر زائد على الذات، موجود في الذات . وهذا هو البحث عن المعقول الصرف .
And its exposition: Among the theologians (mutakallimun) are those who claimed that knowledge (al-ilm) is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the essence of the knower (dhat al-alim), and it has an attachment (ta'alluq) to the known. This speaker affirmed three things: first, the essence; second, the attribute; third, the attachment occurring between that attribute and that known. And among them are those who claimed that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates worldliness (al-alamiyya), then they affirmed an attachment between the knower and the known—and I do not know the exact manner of their doctrines in this. For it is possible they say: the one attached to the known is not knowledge, and it is possible they say: knowledge is the one attached to the known, not worldliness. On these assumptions, they have affirmed four things: the essence, knowledge, worldliness, and the attachment. And it is not unlikely that they affirm the attachment for both knowledge and worldliness. On this assumption they have affirmed five things. As for us, we affirm nothing but two things: the essence and the relation (al-nisba), called worldliness (al-alamiyya), and we claim that this relation is not identical to the essence, but is an additional thing (amr za'id) upon the essence, existing in the essence. This is the investigation of the purely conceptual (al-ma'qul al-sirf).
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparison of different theological positions on the number of entities involved in divine attributes, followed by the author's own position. The author begins by stating that he will now give an exposition (taqrīr) of the views. First, some theologians affirm three things: the essence (dhat), the attribute (sifa) which is knowledge subsisting in the essence, and the attachment (ta'alluq) of that attribute to the known. Second, other theologians claim that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates 'al-alamiyya' (the quality of being knowing/worldliness); they also affirm an attachment between the knower and the known, but the author says he is not sure of the exact details. He posits two possibilities: either the attachment relates to the attribute (knowledge) or to the property (al-alamiyya). On either of these possibilities, they affirm four things: essence, knowledge, worldliness, and attachment. He further suggests it is not unlikely that they affirm attachment for both knowledge and worldliness, resulting in five things. Finally, the author presents his own position: he affirms only two things—the essence and the relation (nisba), which is called al-alamiyya. He insists that this relation is not identical to the essence but is an additional thing (amr za'id) that exists in the essence. He labels this as the purely conceptual inquiry (al-ma'qul al-sirf), distinguishing it from verbal disputes which he will address next.

Correction: Al-Razi affirms that the relation (nisba) is not identical to the essence but is an additional thing existing in the essence.

Verbal disputes about divine attributes and the Mu'tazila position
Al-Razi enumerates four verbal issues about attributes and discusses the Mu'tazila position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's classification of certain debates about divine attributes as merely verbal and the nuanced position of the Mu'tazila regarding attributes.

Core Idea
Not all debates about God's attributes are substantive—some are just about words.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi introduces a key methodological distinction: some theological disputes are verbal, not conceptual. Al-Razi lists four such issues about divine attributes and then addresses a common misconception about the Mu'tazila's stance. Understanding this distinction helps avoid fruitless debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two distinct parts: the four verbal issues and then the sectarian discussion.
2
Identify the four verbal issues
The passage lists four verbal investigations. Find each one and note how Al-Razi phrases each question.
Each issue is introduced with 'أحدها', 'وثانيها', etc.
3
Understand the verdict: verbal, not conceptual
After the fourth issue, Al-Razi declares that all these investigations are verbal, not conceptual. Ask yourself what this means for theological debate.
This is a methodological claim about the nature of these disputes.
4
Examine the sectarian claim and its clarification
Read the claim about philosophers and Mu'tazila denying attributes, then read the parenthetical note that immediately qualifies this. Consider how the note changes the picture.
The note appears to be an editorial insertion or a self-correction, affirming that Mu'tazila do affirm attributes like power.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Think about why Al-Razi (or the commentator) felt the need to include this clarification. What does this tell us about the complexity of theological labeling?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المباحث اللفظية فهي أمور : أحدها: أن تلك الصفات، هل يقال : إنها مغايرة للذات أم لا، بل هي لا نفس الذات ولا غيرها ؟ وثانيها : أن هذه الصفات هل توصف بكونها قدماء أم لا ؟ وثالثها : أن تلك الصفات هل توصف بأنها أعراض أم لا ؟ ورابعها : أنها هل توصف بأنها قائمة بالذات أو حالة في الذات، أو موجودة في الذات، أو يجب الامتناع من كل هذه الألفاظ، إلا أنه يقال : إنها موجودة بالذات ؟ فجميع هذه المباحث لفظية لا معنوية . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المشهور أن الفلاسفة والمعتزلة ينكرون الصفات (٢) وأما الصفاتية فإنهم يثبتونها . (٢) المعتزلة لا ينكرون الصفات . وكيف ينكرون صفة القدرة، وهم يقرأون في القرآن ( إن الله على كل شيء قدير ) ؟ ولهم كتب في علم التوحيد مثل شرح الأصول الخمسة، والمغني، والمجموع في المحيط بالتكليف فيها : إثبات الصفات، وهي للقاضي عبد الجبار وتفسير القرآن للزمخشري فيه تنزيه الله تعالى عن مشابهة المخلوقين. وللقاضي عبد الجبار أيضاً : كتاب تنزيه القرآن من .
As for the verbal investigations (al-mabahith al-lafziyya), they are matters: First: whether those attributes are said to be distinct (mughayira) from the essence or not, rather they are neither the essence nor other than it? Second: whether these attributes are described as eternal (qudama') or not? Third: whether those attributes are described as accidents (a'rad) or not? Fourth: whether they are described as subsisting in the essence (qa'ima bi al-dhat) or inhering in the essence (halla fi al-dhat), or existing in the essence (mawjuda fi al-dhat), or must one abstain from all these expressions, except that it is said: they exist by the essence (mawjuda bi al-dhat)? All these investigations are verbal, not conceptual. If you know this, we say: The well-known view is that the philosophers and the Mu'tazila deny the attributes. (2) The Mu'tazila do not deny the attributes. How could they deny the attribute of power while they read in the Quran 'Indeed, Allah is over all things competent'? And they have books in the science of tawhid such as Sharh al-Usul al-Khamsa, al-Mughni, and al-Majmu' fi al-Muhit bi al-Taklif in which is the affirmation of attributes—and they are by al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar—and the Tafsir of the Quran by al-Zamakhshari in which there is the transcendence of Allah from resemblance to creatures. And al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar also has the book Tanzih al-Quran min...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a classification of certain debates about divine attributes as merely verbal (lafziyya) rather than conceptual (ma'nawiyya). The author lists four such verbal issues: First, whether attributes are distinct from the essence or not, or are neither identical nor distinct. Second, whether attributes are described as eternal (qudama'). Third, whether attributes are described as accidents (a'rad). Fourth, whether attributes subsist in the essence, or inhere in it, or exist in it, or one must avoid all such expressions and simply say they exist by the essence. The author declares all these investigations to be verbal, not conceptual, meaning they revolve around terminology rather than real meaning. He then states the well-known position: the philosophers (falasifa) and the Mu'tazila deny the attributes, while the 'attributes' (sifatiyya, i.e., Ash'aris and others) affirm them. However, a clarifying note follows (likely an editorial insertion or a self-correction) that actually the Mu'tazila do not deny attributes; they affirm attributes like power as evident from Quranic verses, and they have works on tawhid that affirm attributes, such as those by al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Zamakhshari. This note significantly nuances the earlier claim.

Al-Razi shows that some debates about divine attributes are merely verbal, and the Mu'tazila, though often accused of denying attributes, actually affirm them in their own works.

Various Views on the Entities in Divine Attributes
Enumeration of different theological views on entities in divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on the number of entities involved in divine attributes, including views that affirm three, four, or five entities.

Core Idea
Different theologians count the entities in divine attributes differently — from three to five.

In this passage, the author surveys various theological positions concerning what entities are involved when we say God has knowledge. Some affirm a relation between the attribute and its object, and then debate whether that relation attaches to knowledge itself or to the state of being knowing. This leads to different enumerations of the entities. Let's examine these views in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author describes three distinct positions: some affirm a third entity (the relation), some debate the relation's attachment, and others count four or five entities.
Focus on the flow from the initial mention of a third to the four and five entities.
2
Understand the debate about relation
The author notes that it is debated whether 'relation' pertains to knowledge or to the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya). Recognize the two possibilities mentioned.
3
Recognize the four-entity and five-entity views
From the two possibilities, some affirm four entities: essence, knowledge, being knowing, and relation. Others, by affirming relation for both knowledge and being knowing, end up with five entities.
The text does not list the fifth explicitly, but it is an extension of the four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الصفة . وثالثها : التعلق الحاصل بين تلك الصفة وبين ذلك المعلوم . ومنهم من زعم أن العلم صفة توجب العالمية ، ثم إنهم أثبتوا تعلقاً العالم وبين المعلوم ، ولا أعرف كيفية مذاهبهم فيه . فإنه يحتمل أن يقولوا : المتعلقة بالمعلوم لا العلم ، ويحتمل أن يقولوا : العلم هو المتعلق بالمعلوم، لا العالمية . وعلى هذه التقديرات فقد أثبتوا أموراً أربعة : الذات ، والعلم ، والعالمية ، والتعلق . ولا يبعد أن يثبتوا التعلق للعلم وللعالمية أيضاً . وعلى هذا التقدير فقد أثبتوا أموراً خمسة العالمية هي . وأما نحن فلا نثبت إلا أمرين الذات والنسبة ، المسماة بالعالمية ، وندعي أن هذه النسبة ليست نفس الذات، بل هي أمر زائد على الذات ، موجود في الذات . وهذا هو البحث عن المعقول الصرف
Between the attribute. And a third: the relation occurring between that attribute and that known thing. And among them is one who claimed that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya). Then they affirmed a relation between the knower and the known, but I do not know the modality of their doctrines therein. For it is possible that they say: the thing related to the known is not knowledge, and it is possible that they say: knowledge is what is related to the known, not being knowing. On these estimations, they have affirmed four things: the essence, knowledge, being knowing, and relation. And it is not unlikely that they affirm relation for both knowledge and being knowing as well. And on this estimation, they have affirmed five things: being knowing is... As for us, we affirm only two things: the essence and the relation, called being knowing, and we claim that this relation is not identical to the essence, but rather it is something additional to the essence, existing in the essence. And this is the discussion of pure intellectual matters.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage classifies various theological positions on the number of entities involved in the attribute of knowledge. The author, likely ʿAḍud al-Dīn al-Ījī or a commentator, presents different views: some posit a third entity (relation) between the attribute and the object; others debate whether the relation pertains to knowledge or to being knowing. Some then affirm four entities (essence, knowledge, being knowing, relation), and even five if relation is affirmed for both knowledge and being knowing. The author then states the Ashʿarī position: only two things are affirmed — the essence and a relation called 'being knowing' (al-ʿālamiyya). This relation is not identical to the essence but is an additional attribute existing in the essence. The author concludes that this is the discussion of purely intellectual matters (al-maʿqūl al-ṣirf).

The Ash'ari Position on Divine Attributes
Statement of the Ash'ari position on divine attributes emphasizing two entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that only two things are affirmed: the divine essence and a relation called being knowing, which is additional to the essence.

Core Idea
Ash'aris affirm only the essence and an additional relation — not multiple separate entities.

After surveying competing views, the author now presents the Ash'ari stance. This position is characterized by simplicity: only the divine essence and a single additional attribute (a relation) are affirmed. This relation, termed 'being knowing' (al-ʿālamiyya), is not identical to the essence but exists in it as a distinct reality. This is presented as the conclusion of rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The Ash'ari view is introduced with 'As for us' (wa ammā naḥnu).
Notice the contrast with earlier views: they affirm only two things.
2
Identify the two things affirmed
The two things are the essence (dhāt) and a relation (nisba) called being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya). Understand that this relation is the attribute of knowledge in Ash'ari theology.
The term 'nisba' is synonymous with 'taʿalluq' used earlier.
3
Understand the nature of the relation
The passage clarifies that this relation is not identical to the essence, but is something additional (zāʾid) existing in the essence. This is the core of Ash'ari attribution.
This guards against the misconception that Ash'aris reduce attributes to the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الصفة . وثالثها : التعلق الحاصل بين تلك الصفة وبين ذلك المعلوم . ومنهم من زعم أن العلم صفة توجب العالمية ، ثم إنهم أثبتوا تعلقاً العالم وبين المعلوم ، ولا أعرف كيفية مذاهبهم فيه . فإنه يحتمل أن يقولوا : المتعلقة بالمعلوم لا العلم ، ويحتمل أن يقولوا : العلم هو المتعلق بالمعلوم، لا العالمية . وعلى هذه التقديرات فقد أثبتوا أموراً أربعة : الذات ، والعلم ، والعالمية ، والتعلق . ولا يبعد أن يثبتوا التعلق للعلم وللعالمية أيضاً . وعلى هذا التقدير فقد أثبتوا أموراً خمسة العالمية هي . وأما نحن فلا نثبت إلا أمرين الذات والنسبة ، المسماة بالعالمية ، وندعي أن هذه النسبة ليست نفس الذات، بل هي أمر زائد على الذات ، موجود في الذات . وهذا هو البحث عن المعقول الصرف
Between the attribute. And a third: the relation occurring between that attribute and that known thing. And among them is one who claimed that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya). Then they affirmed a relation between the knower and the known, but I do not know the modality of their doctrines therein. For it is possible that they say: the thing related to the known is not knowledge, and it is possible that they say: knowledge is what is related to the known, not being knowing. On these estimations, they have affirmed four things: the essence, knowledge, being knowing, and relation. And it is not unlikely that they affirm relation for both knowledge and being knowing as well. And on this estimation, they have affirmed five things: being knowing is... As for us, we affirm only two things: the essence and the relation, called being knowing, and we claim that this relation is not identical to the essence, but rather it is something additional to the essence, existing in the essence. And this is the discussion of pure intellectual matters.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage classifies various theological positions on the number of entities involved in the attribute of knowledge. The author, likely ʿAḍud al-Dīn al-Ījī or a commentator, presents different views: some posit a third entity (relation) between the attribute and the object; others debate whether the relation pertains to knowledge or to being knowing. Some then affirm four entities (essence, knowledge, being knowing, relation), and even five if relation is affirmed for both knowledge and being knowing. The author then states the Ashʿarī position: only two things are affirmed — the essence and a relation called 'being knowing' (al-ʿālamiyya). This relation is not identical to the essence but is an additional attribute existing in the essence. The author concludes that this is the discussion of purely intellectual matters (al-maʿqūl al-ṣirf).

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the relation (attribute) is not identical to the essence but is something additional existing in it.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that divine attributes are additional relations inherent in the essence, not identical to it, thereby avoiding both identity and multiplicity.

Four Verbal Investigations on Divine Attributes
Enumerates four verbal disputes about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four verbal disputes about divine attributes and why the author considers them purely terminological.

Core Idea
Some debates about divine attributes are about words, not reality.

The author introduces a set of investigations concerning divine attributes that are purely verbal. These four questions—about distinctness, eternity, accidental nature, and inherence—are not substantive but terminological. Understanding this helps us avoid unnecessary theological disputes.

The fourth has multiple options.

Understand the author's verdict
The author says all these are verbal, not conceptual. Reflect on why that matters.
It separates form from substance.

As for the verbal investigations, they are matters: The first: concerning those attributes, is it said that they are distinct from the essence or not, rather they are neither the essence nor other than it? The second: are these attributes described as being eternal (qudamāʾ) or not? The third: are those attributes described as being accidents (aʿrāḍ) or not? The fourth: are they described as subsisting in the essence (qāʾimatun bi al-dhāt) or inhering in the essence (ḥāllatun fī al-dhāt), or existing in the essence, or must we refrain from all these expressions, except that it is said: they exist by the essence (mawjūdatun bi al-dhāt)? So all these investigations are verbal, not conceptual.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage lists four verbal investigations (mabāḥith lafẓiyya) concerning divine attributes. The author classifies them as purely terminological, not substantive. First: whether attributes are said to be distinct from the essence or neither identical nor distinct. Second: whether attributes can be described as eternal. Third: whether they can be called accidents. Fourth: how to describe their relationship to the essence — as subsisting, inhering, existing in it, or refraining from such terms and simply saying they exist by the essence. The author concludes all these are verbal, not meaningful (lā maʿnawiyya).

Two Stations: Proving Attributes Are Additional to the Essence
The author sets up two stations and gives three proofs for the first station.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-station framework for discussing divine attributes and the three proofs showing that knowledge and power are not identical to God's essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are not identical to the essence; they are additional realities.

Before diving into the details of divine attributes, the author clarifies the disagreement among theologians and philosophers. He then outlines two distinct stations of inquiry. The first station focuses on proving that attributes like knowledge and power are not the same as the essence itself. Three rational proofs are presented to establish this point, although the third proof is incomplete in our source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the overall flow from disagreement to two stations to proofs.
The third proof is incomplete; treat it as a fragment.
2
Identify the two stations
Find where the author announces 'there are two stations' and understand what each station addresses.
The first station is covered in this lesson; the second is mentioned but not yet discussed.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof uses the concept of contradictories. Notice how 'knowing' and 'existing' have different contradictories, showing they cannot be identical.
This is based on the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Analyze the second proof
The second proof argues that if being knowing were identical to being existent, then no separate proof would be needed to establish God's knowledge. Since a separate proof is needed, they are distinct.
5
Review the incomplete third proof
Read what remains of the third proof. It likely continues the same theme. Consider why the author might have added a third argument.
The text breaks off mid-sentence, so we cannot fully evaluate it.

If you know this, then we say: The well-known view is that the philosophers and the Muʿtazila deny the attributes, while the attribute-affirmers (al-Ṣifātiyya) affirm them. And the verification in explaining the locus of disagreement is what we mentioned. We say: Here there are two stations: The first: the affirmation of knowledge and power, meaning those specific relations are things additional to the essence. And the second station: the clarification that those attributes called knowledge and power: are they necessary of existence by themselves, or the cause of them is the essence of God Almighty, or is it said that the essence of God necessitates a meaning, and that meaning necessitates these relations and additions? So this completes the discussion in this research. As for the first station, it is to clarify that power and knowledge, according to the interpretation we mentioned, are impossible to be identical to the essence; rather they are two attributes subsisting in the essence. And what indicates the correctness of what we mentioned are proofs: The first proof: Our saying 'knowing' is contradicted by our saying 'not knowing', (and it is not contradicted by our saying 'not existing', and our saying 'existing' is contradicted by our saying 'not existing', and it is not contradicted by our saying 'not knowing') – had existence been identical to knowing, this differentiation would be impossible, because one thing cannot be both a contradictory of something and not a contradictory of it. The second proof: If proof indicates that the world must have an ancient eternal cause necessary of existence by itself, then the mind remains uncertain whether that cause is knowing or not knowing, powerful or not powerful, and it needs a separate proof to establish that He is knowing and powerful. And had His being knowing and powerful been identical to His being existing, ancient, and necessary, it would not be so, because one thing cannot be both known and not known, for combining two contradictories is impossible. The third proof: If we say that the essence of God, existing, is existent... the proposition came out.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the well-known disagreement: philosophers and Muʿtazila deny attributes while Ṣifātiyya affirm them. The author then sets up two stations (maqāmān). The first station is to prove that knowledge and power, understood as specific relations, are additional to the essence. The second station (to be discussed later) concerns whether these attributes are necessary by themselves or caused by the essence. For the first station, three proofs are given. The first proof shows that 'knowing' and 'existing' have different contradictories, so they cannot be identical. The second proof shows that the need for a separate proof to establish that the Necessary Being is knowing and powerful indicates that being knowing is not the same as being existent. The third proof begins but is cut off; it likely continues to show that if essence and attribute were identical, something impossible would follow. The author concludes each proof with the impossibility of combining contradictories.

The Two Stations of Investigating Divine Attributes
The author enumerates two stations in the investigation of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct investigative stations in the debate over divine attributes: the proof of their additional existence and the question of their ontological status.

Core Idea
The discussion of divine attributes unfolds in two logical stations: existence first, then dependency.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational structure of the entire debate on divine attributes. Before any arguments can be assessed, we must first recognize that the inquiry naturally splits into two distinct questions: whether attributes like knowledge and power are extra entities, and whether they are self-subsistent or derived. This passage sets the agenda for the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's use of 'hahuna maqaman' (here are two stations) to signal the framework.
The passage is concise; each station is stated clearly.
2
Identify the two stations
Find the first station: affirmation of knowledge and power as additional to the essence. Find the second station: examining whether those attributes are necessary by themselves, or necessitated directly by the essence, or through an intermediate meaning.
The second station presents three alternative views.
3
Reflect on why the author separates them
Consider why the author considers these two steps distinct. The first is about existence (is there something extra?), the second about causality (how do they exist?). This separation avoids conflating different questions.
This structure is common in Islamic theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق في شرح محل الخلاف ما ذكرنا . قلنا : ههنا مقامان : الأول : إثبات العلم والقدرة ، بمعنى تلك النسب المخصوصة أمور زائدة على الذات . والمقام الثاني : بيان أن تلك الصفات المسماة بالعلم والقدرة. هل هي واجبة الوجود بأنفسها ، أو الموجب لها هو ذات الله تعالى ، أو يقال : ذات الله يوجب معنى ، وذلك المعنى يوجب هذه النسب والإضافات ؟ فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا البحث
And the verification in explaining the locus of disagreement is what we mentioned. We say: here are two stations: First: the affirmation of knowledge and power, meaning those specific relations are matters additional to the essence. The second station: the clarification that those attributes called knowledge and power: are they necessary of existence by themselves, or the necessitator of them is the essence of God Most High, or is it said: the essence of God necessitates a meaning, and that meaning necessitates these relations and additions? So this is the complete discussion in this investigation.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage introduces the investigative framework by distinguishing two stations (maqaman) in the debate about divine attributes. The first station concerns proving that attributes such as knowledge and power are extra entities added to the essence, not identical to it. The second station examines whether those attributes are self-existent (wajibat al-wujud bi-anfusiha) or whether they are necessitated by God's essence, or whether the essence necessitates a middle meaning that then necessitates the attributes. The author states that this covers the whole discussion.

First Proof: Negation Contradiction Shows Distinction
First proof that knowledge and power are not identical to the essence using contradiction of negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that divine attributes such as knowledge and power are distinct from the essence, based on the fact that their negations are different.

Core Idea
If two terms were identical, their contradictions would be identical — but they are not.

In this passage, the author presents a crisp logical proof that the attributes of knowledge and existence (and by extension power) are not identical to the essence. The argument hinges on the simple observation that the negation of 'knowing' is not the same as the negation of 'existing', which would be impossible if they were the same reality. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the logical structure.
2
Identify the two contradiction pairs
The passage mentions that 'knowing' is contradicted by 'not knowing' but not by 'not existing', and 'existing' is contradicted by 'not existing' but not by 'not knowing'. Spot these pairs.
These pairs are crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the conditional argument
If 'existing' were identical to 'knowing', then the two sets of contradictions would be the same. But they are not, so the two cannot be identical. This proves distinction.
This is a logical deduction using the principle of non-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن قولنا : عالم يناقضه قولنا : ليس بعالم ، ( ولا يناقضه قولنا ليس بموجود ، وقولنا : موجود يناقضه قولنا : ليس بموجود ، ولا يناقضه قولنا : ليس بعالم ) (۲) ولو كان كونه موجوداً هو عين كونه عالماً ، لامتنع حصول هذا التغاير ، لأن الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يصدق عليه كونه نقيضـاً له ، وكـونه ليس نقيضاً له .
The first proof: Our statement 'knowing' is contradicted by our statement 'not knowing', (and it is not contradicted by our statement 'not existing', and our statement 'existing' is contradicted by our statement 'not existing', and it is not contradicted by our statement 'not knowing'.) And if His being existent were identical to His being knowing, then this variation would be impossible, because a single thing cannot both be a contradiction to something and not be a contradiction to it.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This is the first proof that knowledge and power are not identical to the essence. It uses a logical argument based on contradiction (naqid). The author notes that the term 'knowing' ('alim) is contradicted only by 'not knowing' (laysa bi-'alim), not by 'not existing' (laysa bi-mawjud). Similarly, 'existing' (mawjud) is contradicted only by 'not existing', not by 'not knowing'. If 'existing' were exactly the same as 'knowing', then the two sets of contradictions would be identical, which is not the case. A single thing cannot have two different contradictions, so the concepts must be distinct.

Key Terms
نقيض
تغاير
عالم
موجود
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes are simply the essence under different names.
Correction: This proof shows that because they have different logical opposites (contradictions), they cannot be identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the logical basis of the first proof?
A) If 'knowing' and 'existing' were identical, the negations of each would also be identical, but they are not.
B) If God is knowing, He must also be powerful.
C) The concept of 'knowing' is more specific than 'existing'.
D) The essence cannot be described by multiple attributes.

Key Takeaway
The different contradictions of 'knowing' and 'existing' prove that they are not identical to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that God's knowledge and existence are distinct. How does this distinction affect your conviction that God's knowledge of your deepest secrets is as real as His power to forgive them? Will you confess something to Him today that you have been hiding?
?
If 'not knowing' is a true contradiction of God's attribute of knowledge, then ignorance cannot be ascribed to Him. Does your prayer life reflect that you believe God knows everything—including your struggles—or do you sometimes act as if He is unaware? What will you change today?
?
The logic forces us to accept that God's attributes are not mere names but real distinctions. Which attribute—such as His justice, mercy, or knowledge—do you tend to ignore or take lightly? How will you honor that attribute in your actions this week?

Second Proof: Attributes Require Separate Evidence
Second proof that attributes are additional because separate proof needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God's attributes are additional to His essence because after proving His existence, the intellect still requires separate evidence for each attribute.

Core Idea
If knowing were identical to existence, no separate proof would be needed.

After establishing that God exists necessarily, the intellect still wonders whether He is knowing and powerful. This uncertainty shows that these attributes cannot be identical to His existence, as otherwise they would be proved automatically. The need for separate proof reveals their distinctness from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the argument flows from a premise to a conclusion.
The passage uses a conditional statement to make its point.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into: (1) After proving existence, the intellect is uncertain about knowledge and power. (2) Therefore, separate proof is needed. (3) If they were identical, no separate proof would be needed. (4) This is impossible because a single thing cannot have contradictory descriptions.
3
Understand the conditional
Focus on the line: 'if His being knowing and powerful were the same as His being existent... then the matter would not be thus.' The proof shows that since the condition does not hold, the identity claim is false.
This is a classic reductio argument.
4
Reflect on the impossibility of contradictions
The passage ends by stating that combining two contradictories (known and not known) is impossible, which supports the need for separate proofs.
This shows the logical necessity of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إذا دل الدليل على أن العالم لا بد له من مؤثر قديم أزلي واجب الوجود لذاته ، فعند هذا يبقى العقل متوقفاً في أن ذلك المؤثر عالم ، أو ليس بعالم . وقادر ، أو ليس بقادر ، ويفتقر في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً قادراً إلى دليل منفصل ، ولو كان كونه عالماً قادراً نفس كونه موجوداً قديماً واجباً . لما كان الأمر كذلك ، لأن الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يصدق عليه كونه معلوماً ، وكونه لا معلوماً . لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال .
The second proof: When the proof indicates that the world must have an eternal, pre-eternal, necessary-of-existence-in-itself effecter, then at this point the intellect remains uncertain whether that effecter is knowing or not knowing, and powerful or not powerful, and it needs a separate proof to affirm that He Most High is knowing and powerful. And if His being knowing and powerful were the same as His being existent, eternal, and necessary, then the matter would not be thus, because it is impossible for a single thing to be both known and not known, as combining two contradictories is impossible.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This second proof argues that after proving the existence of a necessary being (wajib al-wujud), the intellect still pauses and requires a separate argument to establish that this being is knowing and powerful. If being knowing were identical to being existent, then once existence is proven, knowledge would be automatically proven, but that is not the case. Hence, the concepts must be distinct.

Key Terms
مؤثر
قديم
أزلي
واجب الوجود
عقل
دليل منفصل
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God exists, we automatically know He is knowing and powerful.
Correction: This proof shows that after proving existence, the intellect still needs a separate argument to establish knowledge and power, so they are not automatically known.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof use to show that knowledge and power are not identical to existence?
A) The fact that we need a separate argument to prove God is knowing after proving His existence.
B) The fact that God's existence is necessary but His knowledge is contingent.
C) The fact that the universe requires an effecter.
D) The fact that the intellect cannot know both existence and knowledge at once.

Key Takeaway
The need for a separate proof for attributes after proving existence demonstrates that attributes are not identical to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you sometimes assume that because you know He exists, you already know His nature and attributes fully? How does this proof expose that assumption as a mistake? What will you do today to remind yourself that knowing Allah requires active learning, not just passive belief?
?
In your own life, when you become certain of something (like a person's existence or a fact), do you often leap to conclusions about their other qualities without checking? This proof shows the danger of assuming identity between existence and attributes. Where in your relationships or understanding of yourself are you making this error? Commit to one step this week to separate what you know for sure from what you are assuming.
?
The intellect remains uncertain after proving existence. Does your heart feel its own uncertainty about Allah's attributes? Do you act as if you have full knowledge of Him, or do you humbly acknowledge your need for revelation and proof? Ask yourself: What is one attribute of Allah that I need to study deeper, and what practical step will I take today to learn it from the sources?

Third Proof: Informativeness of Propositions
Al-Isfahani's third proof using propositional informativeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third proof uses the informativeness of propositions to demonstrate that God's attributes are conceptually distinct from His essence.

Core Idea
If all statements about God were tautologies, nothing would be informative.

The third proof employs a simple linguistic observation: some statements about God add new information, while others are mere repetitions. By examining why this is so, al-Isfahani demonstrates a key point about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice the two 'if' clauses.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage contrasts two statements: a tautology about existence and a predication of attributes. Identify why the first is uninformative and the second is informative.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does the informativeness of the second statement imply that 'knowing' and 'powerful' are conceptually distinct from 'existent'?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إذا قلنا : إن ذات الله الموجودة موجودة . خرجت القضية عن الإفادة. وإذا قلنا : ذات الله الموجودة : عالم قادر ، كانت القضية مفيدة ، وافتقرنا في التصديق بها إلى الحجة. ولولا أن المفهوم من كونه عالماً قادراً مغاير للمفهوم من كونه موجوداً ، لم يكن الأمر كذلك .
The third proof: If we say 'The existing essence of God exists', the proposition becomes uninformative. And if we say 'The existing essence of God: knowing, powerful', the proposition is informative, and we need a proof to affirm it. And if the concept of His being knowing and powerful were not different from the concept of His being existent, the matter would not be so.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This third proof examines the informativeness of propositions. The statement 'The existing essence of God exists' is a tautology that adds no new information (kharijat 'an al-ifada). However, saying 'The existing essence of God is knowing and powerful' provides new information and requires proof (hujja) to establish its truth. If 'knowing' and 'powerful' were conceptually identical to 'existing', then the second statement would also be trivial. Since it is not, the concepts must be different.

Key Terms
قضية
إفادة
مفهوم
مغايرة
تصديق
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All true statements about God are equally self-evident and need no proof.
Correction: This proof shows that predications like 'God is knowing' add information and require demonstration, unlike tautologies.

Checkpoint
Why does the third proof claim that 'God's existing essence exists' is uninformative while 'God's existing essence is knowing' is informative?
A) Because existence is a necessary attribute but knowledge is contingent.
B) Because the first is a tautology (subject and predicate are the same concept), while the second predicates something different.
C) Because the first statement is false and the second is true.
D) Because existence is proven by senses but knowledge requires revelation.

Key Takeaway
The informativeness of propositions about God reveals that His attributes are conceptually distinct from His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is All-Knowing', do you treat it as a self-evident truth or do you recognise that it requires evidence? How does your answer shape the foundation of your faith?
?
If every statement about God were a tautology, religion would be empty of new information. How does this realization affect your urgency to study kalam proofs for God's attributes?
?
You need a proof (hujja) to affirm that God is 'powerful' beyond His existence. Have you ever sought such proof for any attribute? Commit today to learn one proof from the Qur'an for a single attribute within this week.

The Relational Character of Attributes
Sanusi presents the fourth proof that attributes as relations are distinct from the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relational nature of attributes demonstrates that they are distinct from the divine essence.

Core Idea
Attributes as relations depend on the essence and are not the essence itself.

In this fourth proof, Sanusi argues from the very nature of relations. When we say 'Allah is knowing', a relation (nisba) called knowledge arises between the Knower and the known. This relation is an attribute that depends on the essence and is relative to something else. By considering these characteristics, we see that the attribute must be additional to the essence, not identical to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the argument's flow sink in.
Notice how the proof is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms nisba (relation), sifa (attribute), dhāt (essence), and iftiqār (dependence). Understand how they connect.
These terms form the backbone of the proof.
3
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: Why does dependence on the essence imply that the attribute is not the essence itself? How does the relativity of relations support this?
This step connects directly to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا بينا أنه عالم تحصل النسبة المسماة بالعلم ، لم يحصل كون العالم (۱) عالماً والنسبة بين ذات العالم وبين ذات المعلوم صفة لتلك الذات ، ومفتقرة إليها ، وكيف لا نقول ذلك ، والنسب والإضافات مقبولة بالقياس إلى غيرها ، والذوات القائمة بأنفسها ليست كذلك ، وكل ذلك يوجب التغاير ؟
The fourth proof: We have shown that when he is knowing, the relation called knowledge occurs, and the being of the knower is not [just] the occurrence of the relation, and the relation between the essence of the knower and the essence of the known is an attribute of that essence, and dependent on it. And why would we not say that, while relations and additions are accepted relative to other things, and self-subsistent essences are not so, and all of that necessitates differentiation?
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This fourth proof argues from the relational character of attributes. When we say 'He is knowing', a relation (nisha) called knowledge obtains between the knower and the known. This relation is an attribute (sifa) that depends on the essence and is not the essence itself. Relations are relative (muqbala bi-al-qiyas ila ghayriha), unlike self-subsistent essences. This dependency and relativity show that the attribute is distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
نسبة
إضافة
صفة
ذات
مفتقرة
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are identical to the divine essence or are self-subsistent entities.
Correction: The proof shows that attributes as relations depend on the essence and are relative, thus they are distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
What characteristic of relations does the fourth proof use to argue they are distinct from the essence?
A) Relations are eternal.
B) Relations are dependent on their subjects and are relative.
C) Relations are self-subsistent.
D) Relations are essential to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Attributes as relations are dependent on and relative to the essence, proving they are distinct from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge, do you ever treat it as if it were a separate entity alongside Allah? This proof shows that knowledge is a relation dependent on His essence. How does that reshape your understanding of His oneness? Will you now consciously avoid imagining Allah's attributes as independent realities?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that relations are relative and dependent, while the essence is self-subsistent. Are there ways you rely on your own understanding of Allah's attributes without acknowledging their utter dependence on Him? What would it look like for you to humbly accept that even your knowledge about Allah comes from Him and is not self-standing?
?
If attributes are distinct from the essence yet not separate, how does that affect the way you worship? Could your devotion be directed more toward an attribute (e.g., seeking knowledge) rather than toward Allah Himself? How will you ensure your worship remains directed to the Essence, not to the attributes?
